“THE BULLETIN” BOOK EXCHANCE.

American Authors.
One does not forget Cooper and Hawthorne, Bret Harte and Howells, in
wondering when America is going to produce a first-rank novelist. Poets she has—
essayists, scientists, orators, historians—who may compare with all but the highest
names in old-world literature ; but her Thackeray, her Balzac, her Tolstoi, are still
to seek, ihere seems to be something lacking in the American brain or the Ameri-
can environment, though with such a mingling of rich blood, such a variety of
climate, and such a teeming panorama of effort and incident, their chances would
appear rather in favor of a fuller, stronger intellectual life than is found else-
where. bo far, American culture tends to width rather than depth.
Interest in books and writers is more widely diffused in America than in
Bngiamd, for example; and the humblest American newspapers exhibit a literary
agility and originality which, however ridiculous in extremes, are yet preferable to
ttie comatose parochialism of most English journals outside the centres of population.
But the power of specialisation and concentration seems wanting to American
writers of fiction. They have no back to their heads. They soar high for short
flights, but lack the strength of pinion for sustained struggle. Conceive what memor-
able fruit might be borne were the method of Zola applied in a study of American
hre and character its miseries, its splendours, its industrial strifes, its ferocious
whiil of politics, its stem subjugation of natural forces ! But where is the American
Zola—gifted not only with the mind to plan such a task, but also with the tenacity
and industry to complete if? West, east, centre, north and south, one calls vainly
for an answer. Yet American fictionists have their field of special merit, though it
be not on the highest plane of genius. They are beautiful bellettrists, masters of the
® 01 1 story, which they have lifted to a pitch of dainty delightfulness only reached in
* ranee beside. Their critical novelists of the neo-realistic school, like Howell and James,
it refined to the point of weakness, are invariably graceful and witty. Most of the
authors collected below are artists in words whose style has the delicate savour of a
hothouse flower, and who treat their themes with the sympathy and imagination of
a poet.
This seiies (David Douglas series of American authors) is charmingly printed j
and the books are small and compact, suitable for the pocket. Posted at Is. 3d.
each volume, paper covers ; or cloth extra, 2s. 3d.

They tried to take another referendum in
Switzerland recently, aa to whether doctor’s
advice and medicine should be provided free by
the State, which was to get the necessary money
by monopolising the tobacco-trade. Now, to get
a referendum majority 350,000 votes are needed,
while to initiate the referendum — i.e. , to call for a
vote —it needs 50,000 votes. But after four
months’ canvass only 40,000 could be mustered,
so the scheme fell through. The referendum,
among its other uses, is a fine thing for showing
the real strength of the people who make most
noise, and the frequent difference of opinion
between the mass of voters and their elected
representatives.
All the rage in Paris. Latest Parisian
preventative posted in sealed letter, 1/2 stamps.
National Drug Oo., Russell-st., Mel bourne.
An English professional man, whose family
sent him out'to these beastly kawlinies because he
Was a living illicit-still, turned up again in Sydney
the other day and straightway made for the
editorial apartments of the “leading” paper,
where _ he interviewed the manager and the
managing proprietor. To his appeals they
turned deaf ears. “ We read the papers, Mr.
Jentwun,” they said, with well-intended causticity
»f scorn. _ “Well, what of that? ” “We read the
papers, sir, and we know that you’re a liar, a
drunkard, and a thief, Mr. Jentwun.” “ Well,
what of thatl” was the unabashed rejoinder.
“You will oblige us by quitting these premises,
Mr. Jentwun.” Straightening himself up in a
drunken burlesque of majesty, the deadbeat
replied, in crescendo : “ Yes, I am a liar. Yes,
I’m a drunkard. Yes, I’m a thief. But I won’t
quit these premises. I’ll take a chair, because—
because I feel myself quite at home in your com-
pany, gentleman ! ” What a police-court solicitor
nas been lost in that poor pariah !

From the Antique.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
*J ec ? n * Meeting of Melb. University senate, Mr.
11. u. Higgins moved that the present life-tenure of pro-
fessors should be altered to a five years’ tenure. He
remarked that they had to consider whether it was advis-
able that the Council should be bound in the future to
keep on a professor who might possibly have survived his
usefulness, or that they should be compelled to have a
professor, and to pay him, so long as he was able to be
wheeled up in a bath-chair to say something. The motion
was rejected by a large majority of the senate, who
evidently considered that it was revolutionary in its
tendency and, if accepted, would most likely usher in
blood-stained chaos, Nihilism, and the Day of Judgment.
The academic life was new
To Jones, a student vain,
Who walked one morning through the U-
Niversity Domain.
Eresh from the country he had come,
To wrestle With professordom.
With silent awe he gazed upon
Each college's gray walls —
His great-great grandsire, years agone,
Had studied in those halls —
No sentimentalist was he,
But reverenced hoar antiquity.
When, 10, as he was walking there,
Came slow, the path along,
An aged man with snow-white hair,
And beard a cloth-yard long.
His beard it also was snow-white,
He was an awe-inspiring sight.
The student gazed, astounded, at
This apparition weird,
Clad chiefly in an antique hat,
And in its own vast beard.
He’d never seen the like alive—
This was in nineteen-ninety-five.
He bowed, and said, “ Good morning, sir !”
The ancient answer’d free—
The chirrup of a grasshopper
His voice did seem to be,
So weak it was, and piping, too—
“ Good morning, lad —and who are you?”
His genealogy then Jones,
The while his throat did ache,
Yelled through an ear-trumpet, in tones
That might the dead awake.
The ancient, chuckling, said —“Young sir !
I knew your great-great grandfather.”
“He came here at —I see him yet !
The time—excuse these tears !
When Mr. Higgins tried to get
Our tenure made five years.
Poor Higgins ! Do I blame him ? No !
He died some eighty years ago.”
“ His motion wicked out was bowled,
Though not without some strife,
Thus comes it that professors hold
Their billets still for life.
You see, of course, that I’m alive—
And this is nineteen-ninety-five.”
These old-time memories so amused
The ancient that his head
Wagged horribly ; but he excused
Himself, ere long, and said—
“ You’d like to know my name and rank?
Then know it—l’m Professor Blank !”
The eyes of Jones bulged fearfully,
He felt like going mad,
The ancient grinned, and said, “I’ll see
Your grandson here, my lad.”
Then, like a funeral to a tomb,
He crawled into his lecture-room.
A Yankee perjury—or must Young Aus-
tralia knuckle under at his own game ? IT. More-
land, an American, is credited with having shorn
356 sheep in one day. He worked ten hours, but
still it is a tall tally.
Nervous sufferers will find it worth their
while to peruse Herr Rassmussen’s advt. on page
19 of this issue.
Editor Bulletin, —The Japs, have not by
any means conquered China. They are probably
only at the beginning of their troubles. They
have adopted European arms and drill, but that
alone will not make an army successful in a long
campaign. Old Marshal Saxe said: “An army
goes on its belly like a snake.” He also said ;
“ The whole discipline of war is in the legs and
not in the arms, as some pretend.” Wellington
wrote: “ The success of military operations
depends on supplies. There is no difficulty in
fighting and in finding the means of beating your
enemy with or without loss ; but to gain your
object you must feed.” Those who remember
how Napoleon’s grand army perished in Russia
from sheer want of supplies, though opposed to
an enemy who, even in the extremity of their
misery, could hardly look them in the face;
and how the English army before Sebastopol was
nearly starved, though only seven miles from
its ships, will understand. There is yet
time for the Japs, to learn the same
lesson in Manchuria. The whole country is
intensely hostile to them, if reports are to be
believed, and if it were not it could hardly furnish
subsistence for an army, and subsistence is the
crux of the whole problem. Even so few as
10,000 men will soon eat up a country. They
themselves require something like 10 tons of food
a day. They would hardly have less than 3000
cavalry and artillery horses, which would need
some 20 tons of forage daily. Erom Seoul to the
scene of the reported victory at Ping Yang is 115
miles, or eight marches. Allow one day for
manoeuvring and one for the fight, total 10 days,
we have a consumption of 300 tons of food and
forage. Erom Ping Yang to Moukden in Man-
churia is 250 miles as the crow flies—l 7 marches.
Erom Moukden to Pekin is 355 miles, or 24
marches. In 1860, the allies landed at Tien Tsin,
less than 60 miles, or four marches, from Pekin,
and though they utterly crushed all the force the
Chinese could bring against them, and took the
capital, they could not advance further even if
their terms of peace had been refused. The
strength of China is its inertia, which is invincible,
and the Japs, will find it so.— Portfire.

SATURDAY, NOVEMBER 10, 1894.
Settling the People on the Land.
A year or two ago the desire to settle the
people on the land assumed an aggravated
form in Victoria and N.S. Wales. In each
of these provinces nearly half the population
is centred in one big city created mainly on
borrowed money. Neither of these cities
can show any legitimate reason for its being,
or any visible means of support which can
bear inspection. They exist partly because
they are the seats of Government; partly
because the whole railway system of their
respective provinces has been centred there
so as to drag the entire commerce of the
community into one unnatural focus ; partly
on miscellaneous finance, because commerce
having been directed there, they naturally
became the headquarters of all financial
operations; partly by the unnatural and
artificial increase in their own ground-values,
by the erection of big buildings with bor-
rowed money to keep pace with the unwhole-
some inflation of the ground-values aforesaid ;
and partly on a few small industries, and their
own alleged glorious future. Buttheindustries
of Melbourne are so few, and those of Sydney
so much fewer, and the commerce of both
cities is so small in comparison with the
numbers of people dependent upon it,
and there is such a total absence of all
reasonable means of existence for the
population, that the existence of these
bloated capitals may be fairly set down as
owing, in the main, to the influx of burrowed
money from Europe. No other country in
the world has borrowed so much foreign
capital in proportion to population, and no
other Country has anything like so immense
a proportion of its population concentrated
in one city, as either Victoria or New South
Wales. Therefore, there is at least some
semblance of reason in regarding the bor-
rowed money as the cause and the useless,
bloated, overgrown metropolis as the effect.
* * •
As the natural result of this state of things
there is, among the workers of Australia, an
alarmingly small proportion of actual pro-
ducers, and when recently the bottom fell
out of the great borrowing industry the
effect was immediately apparent. This last
remark is not to be taken in a literal sense,
for, directly or indirectly, this country still
borrows nearly all she can get. But the
debt now amounts to so enormous a sum
that all the foreign capital which finds its
way to Australia does not equal the outgo in
the shape of interest, and even the most
vigorous borrowing adds nothing to the
available cash in circulation. And with the
sudden stoppage of many industries which
existed very largely on foreign capital—with
the practical suspension of railway construc-
tion, the shrinkage of foreign commerce and
banking, the paralysis of the building trade
because the cities of Australia have already
been built altogether too much, the vanish-
ing away of many millions in paper wealth,
and the consequent decline in the demand
for all kinds of luxuries —every trade, save
that of the man who manufactures actual
necessaries, or who produces actual neces-
saries from the soil, was found to be
grievously overstocked. Victoria and New
South Wales proved, at this crisis, to have
about the smallest existing percentage of
direct producers in the world, and, in pro-
portion to their population, one of the
largest and most insoluble social problems on
the face of the earth.
* * *
Then the desire to settle the people on the
land became an epidemic among Australian
politicians. It was necessary, by some
means or other, to turn as many as possible
of the multitude of non-producers into pro-
ducers in the shortest possible time, and
therefore the idea of founding labour settle-
ments was conceived by city politicians who
had a vague conviction that agriculture was
an instinct, and that the growing of veget-
ables came by nature. In some cases the
same politicians were also troubled by a
vague assurance that the broken-down clerk,
or other retrenched city worker, could live
on a soil which the practical agriculturist
would abandon in despair, so the labor
settlements were often dumped down on
remote patches of sterile soil, on recks and
swamps and all manner of Goo-forsaken
locations ; and whosoever desired was sent
out there with a spade and an assurance of a
small Government subsidy, and bade to
settle down and prosper. They never did
prosper for many reasons. The story of the
Pitt Town labour settlement, where nothing
seems to grow except the State subsidy, and
where the whole cash return from a good-sized
community has been £5O or so, realised
by the sale of timber, has already been told.
And the utter failure of the whole village
settlement scheme in Victoria has now
become so apparent that the hollow pretence
of settling the people on the land can hardly
be kept up any longer.
• « #
MHntyre, Minister for Lands under the
Patterson regime, was the most enthusiastic
apostle of the village settlement scheme
in all Australia. The colony which he
planted in the Koo-wee-rup swamp was pro-
bably the biggest effort of his genius. By
his orders surveyors waded through this
dreary morass often up to their waists in
mud, and patiently pegged out allotments
on which 1000 or so of the Melbourne un-
employed were settled with a subsidy of £l5
per head and the Ministerial blessing. Most
of the allotments are merely five acres of
bog, in which occasionally a bony and lone-
some cow finds precarious foothold. Hardly
anything grows at Koo wee-rup, or will grow
until an immense and elaborate drainage
system is created, and the settlers who toil
miserably at singeing damp scrub, and feebly
attempting to plant potatoes in the slime and
slush, have degenerated into a hopeless race
with no ambition save to keep alive and to
find, if possible, a dry spot for standing-
room. The other Victorian village settle-
ments differ from Koo-wee-rup mainly
because some of them are rock instead of
morass; they are all, whether the soil is
good or bad, about equally hopeless,
and there is not even a semblance of
prosperity among any of them. And even in
Maoriland, where better land was provided
and more money expended, and where the
operations were conducted with an intense
earnestness which ought to have commanded
success, the village settlement scheme has
not anything like repaid its cost. The best
that can be said for the best village settle-
ment of the lot is that it has failed quietly
and respectably ; and the worst that can be
said about the worst collapse in the whole
dismal category is that it is a national
disgrace. Whether the land was good or
bad, or whether the other conditions were
favourable or the reverse, the result has
always been failure, more or less definite.
* * *
The same result, or want of result, has
attended the attempted fishing settlement in
Bass Straits, the great scheme of the Dlbbs
Ministry to turn the Sydney unemployed
into gold-miners, and every other effort to
settle the work less city population on the
land, or anywhere else. For many years
the policy of Australia’s foolish and,miser-
ably improvident politicians was to build up
a huge urban population, and the conse-
quences of that population have apparently
come to stay, for this generation at all
events. A large proportion of the hungry
and vagrant classes for whom there is no
longer room in the cities can’t be settled on
the land any more than they can in the
upper atmosphere. Agriculture —as the
city-bred politicians of Australia seem utterly
unable to realise —doesn’t come by instinct
any more than does watchmaking, or the
manufacture of boots or saddlery, or any
other trade. To the man of strength and
industry, with a small capital, the necessary
training, reasonably good land, and a market
that is not too remote, it yields about as
good returns as any other pursuit ; but with-
out these essentials it yields little, or
nothing, or less than nothing. And every
other productive occupation, without the
needful training, yields just about as much.
» # #
To those politicians who are seeking an
immediate remedy for the unemployed diffi-
culty The Bulletin respectfully presents the
theory that there is no remedy under
the sun. Australia, through its famous
borrowing policy, has more big city
in proportion to its population than any
( her civilised country. It has also, as the
natural consequence, far more clerks, shop-
men, and similar unproductive inhabitants
than the average. Unless it can be born
again and live its garish past all over once
more, there is no eqrthly probability that it
will find employment for anything like the
same number of unproductive inhabitants
again for many years to come. The problem,
so far as concerns the country at large, may
be settled by fostering manufactures and
agriculture under a wise policy of Pro-
tection, by economy, no more borrow-
ing, an entirely new land system, and
a more equitable division of the public
burdens ; but, so far as concerns a large
proportion of the individual sufferers, it will
never be settled. There is abundant hope
for their sons and grandsons, but that is all.
The infatuated extravagance of the past has
created a large class of workless citizens
who, to tell the unfeeling truth in the
plainest words, are fit for no remunerative
purpose under the new conditions which
have arisen, and who will only make first-
class funerals. What the State should do
with them, or for them, The Bulletin,
after much hard consideration of the pro-
blem, doesn’t pretend to know. What it
does know is that they can’t be successfully
settled on the land, and that even if they
could the mercantile and legal politicians of
Australia are blankly ignorant as to how to
settle them there.
The Coming Australian.
“ In certain branches of science,” wrote George
Eliot, “we can ascertain our personal equa-
tion, the measure of difference between our own
judgment and the average standard.” We can ;
but few people ever do. This “personal equa-
tion ’’—the idiosyncrasy of thought due to bias,
or training, or circumstance, which marks
every man’s departure from the norm—generally
influences those opinions to which beyond all
others their holder is wedded, and frequently
those in which he is surest of impartiality.
Every observer unconsciously reduces things to
his own measure of value and when he goes
farther, and reduces the measure itself to lan-
guage, the result is generally a very far and
faint approximation to the total truth. It is
vitiated, firstly, by the imperfection of the
observer’s mind ; and, secondly, by the incapa-
city of language to portray exactly what is in
his” mind. Thus the critics of Australian charac-
ter whose dicta were collected in a recent Bulle-
tin have all their weak points, and only in their
unanimity'lies anything approaching certainty.
In every case we must eliminate the personal
equation. Marcus Clarke, for example, dis-
cussed all Australia from a Victorian point of
view ; and it is true, as Adams says, that “ the
ignorance of Victorians concerning the facts of
daily existence in Queensland is often only one
shade less than that of full-blown British ‘new-
chums.’ ” Adams himself was an invalid with
nerves strung by disease—wonderfully keen,
but apt to lay too much stress on trifles which
appealed strongly to his sensitive brain, and
better at description than at generalisation.
But he is always in earnest, always to be taken
seriously. Clarke loved an epigram too
dearly to bother about strict accuracy, and he
wrote rather to make a smart article than to
utter sound criticism. One never quite knows
how far he meant his epithets.
# * *
Clarke’s view of the characteristics of “ the
future Australian race ” simply exaggerates his
impressions of Victorian characteristics when
his essay was written—as when he sets down
“ a form of Presbyterianism ” as the religion of
the coming nation. The pretence of profundity
with which he commences his argument was
probably used, as Poe used his science, to give
“ artistic’verisimilitude” to the play of fancy.
Clarke starts with the axiom that two things,
food and climate, determine the quality of a
race. He gives quasi-historical instances to
show that facts correspond with what might be
deduced from a knowledge of these conditions.
Then he considers what will be the food and
climate of Australia, and draws the quite unwar-
ranted (even from his premisses) conclusion that
Northern Australia will have a luxurious civilisa-
tion like that of ancientEgyptand Mexico, while
Southern Australia will be a republic—with the
intellectual capital in Victoria, the governing
capital in New Zealand, and the social capital
at Sydney. Then he sums up Victorian ten-
dencies in 1877, transfers them to the “ future
Australian race,” and closes with an audacious
climax which clinches the whole beautifully.
And there you are. Had Clarke taken the
pains to fill in his sketch with Poe’s minute-
ness of detail, it would have been remarkably
convincing. As it is, it only strikes one as
wonderfully clever.
# * *
For, in the first place, no historical analogues
will exactly fit our case. Race-quality is not
altogether a matter of food and climate. X t
any historical comparison, much must be
allowed for racial heredity ; much for differ
ence in complexity of civilisation ; much i' ot '
the constant evolution of humanity. You
cannot argue that because part of Egypt is j n
the tropics, part of India in the” tropics'
part of Mexico in the tropics, and par f
of Australia in the tropics, therefore Australia
after the lapse of 2000, 1000, or 600 years’
will repeat the history of Egypt, of India’
or of Mexico. Yet this is practically Clarke's
argument (so far as he has any argument) on
the side of climate. On the side of food Clarke
forgot what a change has been made by modern
methods of transport, as compared with the
days when commerce was all but unknown. Xhe
future Australian will have at his command Hie
food of the world ; though his own continent
indeed, is capable of varying his diet inde-
finitely. Clarke’s brilliant literary edifice in
fact, is no sooner looked at seriously than it
falls to pieces. It must be taken as it was pro-
bably meant—as a dazzling impressionist tour-
de-force, suggestive rather than authoritative
and perennially charming.
• # *
Clarke’s logical method, so far as he had
any, was bound to lead him astray. It i s 0 f
course, as hopeless to attempt to dogmatise on
the ultimate destinies of Australia as it is to
forecast the career of an infant twelve months
old. But when one attempts, what is quite
possible, to ascertain the probable drift of our
national character in a few years’, or a few score
years’ time, if present conditions continue little
modified, it is wholly unprofitable to lay the
country on the Procrustean bed of history, and
stretch or lop her limbs until they appear
to fit a set of circumstances totally
different from ours. Historical illustrations
of course, will always have value in so far as
they relate to cardinal principles of human
nature. A hungry man, nowadays, will try
and satisfy his hunger as hungry men did at
the siege of Jerusalem ; an Australian mob
differs little in essence from an Athenian mob;
the cleric who sells heaven to credulous wor-
shippers waxes just as fat as his Egyptian pre-
decessor of 3000 B.c. But Australians will
never be Jews, or Athenians, or Egyptians.
Once the world turns down a leaf, it never
recurs ; and though the writing on our page be
similar to much that has been written before
us, yet similarity is not identity. We must
dree our own weird.
• * •
Thus, in any conjectural horoscope of the
coming Australian, we must start from things
as they are, not from things as they have been.
History may be a light to our path, but it can
never be the path itself. With patient obser-
vation of facts for a few years more, sufficient
should be known of Australian character as
modified by Australian conditions to enable
general principles, the result of alien experi-
ence, to be aptly applied. But, without
preliminary induction, deduction is a tool
which slips in the hand. If thought-
ful men all over Australia, especially
school-teachers and doctors, were to re-
cord their observations of the physique, the
intellect, the morals, and the habits, of native-
born Australians as compared with their for-
bears or with natives of other districts or
provinces ; and if some such body as the
Association for the Advancement of Science
could regularly tabulate and publish these
observations; the philosopher of 25 years hence
would be enabled to speak with some authority
as to our future, and his conclusions would be
of the greatest possible value. The Bulletin
asks its correspondents all over Australasia to
forward information of characteristic national
developments as foreshadowed in individuals,
or classes, or localities, always providing that
they be chronicled with brevity and brains, f

fatuously certify to having; worn three or sixteen

Die beastly “ colonial ” hat that goes green—in
Die back-blocks it is the imported hat. The
mainstay of some of the anonymities in the
discussion is that people who have signed their
name s are interested parties. The crux of the
nia Der was reached when the Denton hat-mills,
through their chairman, offered to accept a
practical test of their hats, whose success they
would back by a wager. One of the principal
anonymities thereupon declared that he was
opposed to gambling as well as to Protection ;
but, seeing that the all-round superiority of
Freetrade is an axiomatic truth, it is difficult to
see where (betting, as the Foreign-trader would
be, on a dead certainty) the gambling comes in.
However, the local agents of Woodrow put
ou t a challenge at this juncture. They would
compare Denton mill hats purchased at
random in Melbourne with Woodrow hats,
a s Woodrow makes only best grade hats, and
Denton Mills make every grade required by
Die market, this challenge was not suitable for
acceptance. But the Deaton Mills are willing
to pit their best-grade hats against the world,
However, it is not the quality of Australian
bats that is the thorn in the Freetrade side.
Xhe Denton Mills pay. Behold everything!
Agriculture has fallen on its knees to the
Government, ; wool has fallen lower still, the
“ natural industries ” are drooping, capitalists
are shaking ; but this economic error, Protec-
tion, produces a thing that pays —in bad times,
too.

the hardship of the present men are laying up

of Free Raid —to know the land as the source
of the nation’s wealth, not as a tool to enrich
confidence-men —to know that even the wealth
in the gift of Protection will be torn from the
workers, if rogues have sway.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
That Registration Clause—Agaih.
New South Wales Parliament has definitely
affirmed that clause in the new Land Bill
which decrees that, in future, any person
taking action against a selector to recover a
debt must first deposit £2OO with the Treasury
as a guarantee that his debtor will not be fired
out homeless into the world. As a specimen of
legislative incapacity this provision is almost
unique. Its avowed object is the humane and
altogether commendable one of making the selec-
t ir'shomesteadinviolableas againsthiscreditors,
and so far as the object is concerned the only
possible objection is that it doesn’t go nearly far
enough. Not only the selector’s, but every-
body else’s home should be equally inviolable,
and, along with the home—in the agriculturist’s
ease —should go sufficient land for the main-
tenance of himself and his family. But the
i ew law, after struggling feebly round the
question in circles, doesn’t make the selector’s
home inviolable, after all. In the first place, the
bucolic person has to register himself before
he comes under the provisions of the Act, and
thus furnish profitable employment to a lawyer
in filling up the necessary documents. New
South Wales Assembly is full of lawyers, and
they are all hungry after fees, so the simple
and obvious plan of protecting every selector’s
homestead without the cumbrous system of re-
gistration was entirely ignored. Then even the
registered selector’s home isn’t protected ; the
only protection is that his creditor, when he
turns him out, must pay him £2OO with which
to get another home. The expense and loss of
time involved in finding a new farm, and re-
moving thither, will absorb a good part of the
£2OO in any case, to say nothing of the fact that
the evicted selector will often fall among land-
agents by the way, and be fleeced of his little
pile altogether; and the alternative device of
leaving him undisturbed in his old residence is
so much simpler and more effective that it is
difficult to imagine how the Reid Government
managed to avoid thinking of it. Further,
the new law is an elaborate legal swindle
upon the creditor. It was originally intended
to enact that there must be left to the debtor,
when he is sold up for his liabilities, a house
and land to the value of £2OO, and to get at
this idea it is enacted instead that the creditor,
before he even sues for his money, must deposit
£2OO with the Treasury as a guarantee to this
effect. The creditor may be a miserable little
storekeeper who never owned £2OO at one time,
er anything like it, in his life, and he may only
have a claim of £5O or so against a selector
with £2OOO worth of property. Nevertheless,
he must find an impossible sum of money as a
guarantee that he won’t turn-out his wealthy
debtor to starve, before he can even attempt to
recover his unpaid bill. The whole device is an
unspeakable bungle. Every creditor should
have a right to sue a selector and get a judg-
ment against him in the same way as he is
allowed to sue anyone else. Having
got his judgment he should be allowed
to recover his money in the ordinary
way, with the proviso that under all
circumstances the debtor’s homestead, land to
a certain value fixed by law, agricultural im-
plements, and stock to a certain limited value,
shall be inviolate whether registered or not.
As for the proposed deposit, of £2OO, it merely
makes it impossible for the poor creditor to
recover anything under any circumstances, and
it serves no other purpose except to prove how
impossible it is for a legislature of lawyers to
go straight at the simplest proposition, so long
as there is any possible chance of going at it
sideways.
Conjugal Collusion.
In Melbourne recently a certain rule nisi was
granted in divorce, the husband being the
applicant. There was no appearance on the
part of the wife, and no opposition. Later on,
however, it transpired that the husband and
wife had arranged, in a friendly way, before
the application was made, that there should be
no opposition, and the Judge thereupon de-
nounced the case as one of “ the grossest collu-
sion between the parties,” and refused to grant
the final decree. Consequently the parties con-
tinue married, and are likely to remain that
way for the rest of their lives, for the person
who is once found guilty of collusion is lost.
Of all the many derangements of the law its
rooted dislike to collusion is about the most
insane and the most unaccountable. If only
one party wants a separation, and the other
fights tooth and nail against it, and they pay
immense fees to a crowd of hungry lawyers, and
fill the papers with loathsome details of im-
morality, and tales of drunkenness, cruelty,
and sordid domestic brawls, the court generally
grants the relief requested. Or, if both
parties want a separation, and they both
apply for it, and each one fights to get it
and yet to prevent the other party getting it,
and the legal bills are something enormous,
and instead of one dirty story being told there
are two, and the legal atmosphere is filled with
perjury and lascivious information, and the
witnesses are examined at great length by
comic and dirty-minded lawyers, and both
parties are ruined, and their characters are
utterly blasted, and they are both sternly
reprimanded from the Bench, then also the
separation is granted in the great majority of
cases. But, if both husband and wife want a
divorce, and they have sufficient decency to
suppress the horrible details, and to do with-
out the cross-examination by the comic lawyer,
and to save the ruinous legal costs, by the
simple process of one party making an unop-
posed application and telling just as much of
the unpleasant tale as is absolutely necessary,
then the law heaves reproaches at them and
refuses to have anything to do with them.
The judicial objection to the collusion is
apparently based on the fact that it suppresses
the unclean details and deprives the lawyers
of their fees. The law lives mainly on fees and
unclean details, and collusion is an outrage on
the bread and cheese of the Bar.
Mrs. Mellon’s “Bosom Friends.’*
Mrs. A. G. Edwards, president of the Sydney
Spiritualists’ Association, who was present at
the Mellon exposure stance, but has held
her tongue out of compassion for the dis-
credited Mrs. Mellon and for the sake of “ the
Cause,” now comes forward to say she has
decided to make a clean breast of everything.
Mrs. Edwards, whose reputation is that of an
inherently honest and sincere woman, states
that when the rude, wicked, and unchivalrous
sceptic, Mr. Henry, collared the medium (the
same medium so hysterically belauded by
Stead, of the Review of Reviews !), she
distinctly saw the mask, the white drapery,
and the rest of the properties; and, when the
gentlemen were turned out, she went to the
bowled-out medium, who was squatting on the
floor, and—
Sticking up from the bosom of her dress, which was of
V shape, I saw a lump of coarse, dark hair, the portion
visible being fully IJin. one way and nearly an inch longer
the other. I pushed it down her bosom, distinctly feeling
it to be hair.
Mrs. Edwards and another lady then assisted
to put on Mrs. Mellon’s “ upper petticoat and
skirt,” which had unaccountably fallen off
along with her shoes and stockings in the pro-
cess of materialisation; and all the time Mrs.
Mellon persisted in squatting. When all the
other ladies had gone, leaving Mrs. Mellon still
glued to the floor in the attitude of the cele-
brated jumping-frog, and only Mrs. Edwards
remained, the medium at last consented to
arise.
She then stooped, and raising her petticoats slightly in
front, she drew something from the back, and fastened
whatever it was under the skirt with two or three pins.
She then put her shoes and stockings on, and left the
cabinet.
Then when at last Mrs. Edwards wrote to
Mrs. Mellon for an explanation, Mrs. M,
brought her explanation in person :
She said she wished to explain everything to me. She
wore the same body as that she had on on the night of
the sdance—an evening dress body, V-shaped—and said
to me, ,l You know how thin I am. Like other ladies I
am obliged to pad,” and then pulled out of her bosom an
uncovered mass of black hair, which she said she wore as
a false bust. I indignantly pointed out the absurdity of
the statement, saying that no woman would wear un-
covered hair as a breast-pad. She further stated that the
pinning of her petticoat at the bottom was in consequence
of the strings having broken at the waist, and she pinned
it there to prevent it falling off! I refused to believe
her, and she left the house.
Having thus decided that the explanation was
thin as well as the bust, Mrs. Edwards lost no
time in getting the awful load off her chest.
The public have now an opportunity of infer-
ring from this example how much the devoted
followers of any religion are tempted to suppress,
against, the interests of the public, for the good
of “ the Cause.” Continues Mrs. Edwards :
I was so horrified at the time at the prospect of
exposure and the fear that she might be searched that,
on the spur of the moment, I said, “Give me anything
you have to hide,” in reply to which she said, “No, no ;
I have nothing but at my suggestion she pinned the
front of her dress higher.
Mrs. Edwards also states that, before the
historic grab :
When “Josephine” appeared, whom I had always sus-
pected to be Mrs. Mellon herself, the form stooped to
take the flowers with a motion which I recognised as
distinctly characteristic of Mrs. Mellon.
Nevertheless, had there been no grab, and had
Mr. Henry and others not recorded what they
saw, there is no reasonable ground for believing
that Mrs. Edwards’ sense of duty to the public
would have outweighed her suspicions,
amounting to certainty, and the compassion
she not unnaturally felt for her own amour
propre and for Mrs. Mellon. However, better
late than never. For what we have all received
at last let us be truly thankful. The cat is out
of the bag. The potato is cooked. Mrs. Mellon
can now only resuscitate her mediumistic
mana by raising and reincarnating the ghost of
the Marble Man.
The Horsehair Corporation.
The Barristers’ Regulation Bill, which Miller,
M.L.A., proposes to introduce in New South
Wales Assembly, provides, among other things,
that no barrister shall practise before a Judge
who is his blood relation. If it passes, this pro-
vision will be a hard knock to one or two bar-
risters who practise almost exclusively before
their relations on the bench, but it will have
the merit, at all events, of making justice look
a good deal more pure than it looks at present.
There is no earthly reason to suppose that any
Australian Judge corruptly favours his own
barristerial relative at the expense of justice,
but when a barrister gets plenty of briefs in the
court over which his relative presides, and very
few anywhere else, the obvious inference is that
low-minded attorneys fully expect the Judge
to decide in his relative’s favour as often as
possible—or to unconsciously give the relative
tips which help his argument. Another pro-
vision not nearly so wise is that no British bar-
rister shall be admitted to practise in N.S. Wales
except by going through the ordinary local
examinations after the usual course of study,
until Britain reciprocates by allowing New
South Wales barristers the same privileges as
its own horsehair brigade now enjoys in Aus-
tralia. As things are at present, the local
barrister has to toil for a number of years at
learning the statutes of Botany Bay and passing
heavy exams, before he is admitted, but the
English barrister who knows nothing at all of
Cornstalk law, and is useless for local purposes,
is admitted without any question. Mr. Miller
doesn’t propose to suppress this custom because
it is a fraud upon the public, but merely
because Britain doesn’t admit the New
South Wales lawyer to exhibit an exactly
similar variety of ignorance in the British
courts, and to perpetrate a similar fraud
on the British public. His eye is fixed
solely on the fact that the N.S.W. Bar is ex-
posed to unfair competition, and when things
are made straight so far as the Bar is concerned
the poor old public may take its chance as
heretofore. There is nothing in the Bill to
enable the law to get at the solicitor who em-
bezzles the barrister’s fee, or to get at the
barrister who takes his fee but doesn’t appear,
and allows his luckless client’s case to be lost
through his neglect. The visible purpose of
the measure is merely to make the Bar a closer
corporation than it is at present, which is
doubtless a matter of interest to the Bar, though
of no perceptible benefit to any one else.
It is gratifying to know that the Wairarapa—
which was wrecked while sprinting at its best
pace along a narrow channel on a dark, foggy
night—was not sacrificed, along with a hundred
lives, through any desire on the part of the
U.S.S. Co. to keep up its speed-records at all
hazards. The company, it appears, gives its
captains strict orders to consider safety first,
and make speed a mere secondary considera-
tion. But so long as the desire exists to keep
ahead of a rival, speed must be a very impor-
tant consideration, and the captain who is most
certain of keeping his billet, at a time when
billets are few and captains many, is the one
who makes fast passages and also escapes acci-
dent. So he goes in for combining the two
advantages until the smash comes, and while he
is waiting for the smash he is a splendid evi-
dence that velocity can be conjoined with im-
munity from accident, and other captains have
to live up to his standard or be suspected of
inefficiency. The rule that safety is the
first and speed only the second con-
sideration is of very little account, for
no ship is absolutely safe at sea, and, therefore,
the question of how much speed should be
sacrificed for the sake of an indefinite quantity
of security is one which nobody can answer.
When a captain takes no avoidable risk and
makes a slow passage it is impossible to prove
that he would have been wrecked if he
had made a fast one ; and when a captain
makes a record passage in safety it is
impossible to prove that he will be wrecked
when he tries to do it again. Unless some pro-
vision can be made whereby the reckless cap-
tain will be sunk every time, and the careful
one will always get through in safety, or unless
things can be so fixed that a captain’s great
object will be to make slow passages, it is
hardly worth while issuing instructions such, as
those of the U.S.S. Co., at all.
The Medical Practitioners’ Bill, now before
N.S.W. Parliament, is one of the very few
measures which goes to the point without any
needless circumlocution and arrives at its
destination with reasonable velocity. It pro-
vides that any person professing to be a legally-
qualified physician or surgeon, when he isn’t
one, may be fined .£5O for his first offence, and
£5 for every day during which the offence is
continued, with the alternative of a year’s gaol.
The quack is to be allowed to practise as here-
tofore, with the proviso that in his advts. and
on the name-plate which he is required to fix
on his big front door he must add the words
“ not registered.” Consequently, that section
of the public which prefers the quack can
have him, and that other section which
doesn’t want to be treated by a quack will
have the opportunity hitherto denied it of
distinguishing between him and the other
man. A third clause provides that no
practitioner shall trade under any name save
his own. When a successful quack retires he
generally sells his business to another man who
takes his name and passes himself off as the
original ; consequently in New South Wales
even the quack is often bogus, and the public
is imposed upon, first by the man who falsely
professes to be a qualified M.D., and, secondly,
by the man who falsely pretends to be the per-
son who did the original imposition. Some-
times it even happens that one quack buys the
practice of another, and puts both names on his
door, and represents himself as whichever
party the patient wants, and if the Medical
Practitioners’ Bill abolishes this gentleman in
both his capacities it will do good service to a
lame and halt and diseased community.

F. Kidd, the successor of Lane as chairman o 1
the Rue Australia settlement, writes express-
ing a pathetic surprise that “some of those
prominent in organising the movement have not
yet put in an appearance,” and explaining that
he and many others joined the settlement
“ understanding that the propagandists would
help with the management of the colony.”
Then he goes on to express a kind of mournful
hope that Mr. Casey “ will be able to fully
explain away everything, and that further
shiploads of emigrants will follow.” Poor F.
Kidd ! The chances are that corn will grow on
a gallows before any more of the prominent
organising jawsters will turn up in Paraguay,
and there will be no more shiploads of emigrants
between now and the day of judgment. Even
if Gilbert Casey could explain away all South
America it would make no difference, simply
because the public enthusiasm is as dead as
John Knox, and as flat as the beer that
was uncorked in the Middle Ages. F.
Kidd further reports that the settlement
is getting along steadily, but that some
of the men will soon be leaving. And even
supposing the colony doesn’t collapse through
famine they will most likely continue to leave
one by one from mere want of enthusiasm and
because they are tired of seeing each other,
until there is only one man left. There is
almost always a Last Man in such cases, and he
grows his hair long, and becomes a monomaniac,
and goes round gibbering and mowing to him-
self until he dies at a good old age and with
his talons about six inches long. Being the
Last Man there is no one to bury him, but
generally his faithful dog eats him in course of
time, and then mourns over its lost master until
it dies also, and that ends the show.
The late James Anthony Froude was parti-
cularly strong —not to say putrid—on the Irish
question. He looked upon the Irish race—the
non-Protestant, nou-Anglitied natives —as “a
danger and a torment to every country to
which they emigrate.” He believed in tha
Cromwellian policy of ruthless and wholesale
extermination to the glory of God and the
profit of the saints. Impaling and flogging
were methods highly esteemed by him for the
encouragement of truth in tfie Irish peasantry.
The proposed substitution—in 1719 —of castra-
tion for the mere branding with hot irons of
unregistered priests is explained and excused
by him. The fiercest barbarities of the English
and Scotch settlers are defended in that
ghastly book “The English in Ireland,”
whilst the frantic struggles of the ignorant and
brutalised peasants are painted in the most
lurid colors and condemned in the strongest
language. At their best, according to Froude,
the Irish are emotional windbags led by wind-
bags ; at their worst, they are Calibans led by
devils. To do him justice, he has one good
word to say about the miscreants. “ The ex-
ceptional and signal purity of Irish Catholic
women of the lower class, unparalleled pro-
bably in the civilised world . . . must be
attributed,” he admits, “ wholly and entirely
to the influence of the Catholic clergy.” Charac-
teristically, this testimony is borne in a little
footnote to a voluminous text about the wicked-
ness of priests, who certainly deserved brand-
ing, if not castration. Froude did as much
harm as he knew how to Ireland. But Irish-
men are forgiving. They would probably at-
tend his funeral with pleasure, if they could.
A FEW of the weak points about Spiritualism
are these : No medium has ever called up a
spirit that had a single bright or original
observation to offer about his present state.
The brightest thing that was ever said by a
spirit was that he was well and happy, and
that his grandmother was enjoying herself. No
medium has ever called up a spirit which spoke
a language which the medium didn’t speak
himself. Also, no medium has ever evoked a
ghost which gave any valuable or definite in-
formation about the past. If Spiritualism was
a reality, it could cast more light on the dark
places of history than any other agency. Think
of even a few of the things which the world is
yearning to know—why Grouchy muddled
away his splendid chance on the day before
Waterloo ; who were the Man in the Iron
Mask, the alleged Sebastian of Portugal,
Cagliostro, and Smerdis the Magian ; when
was Christ born ; what became of Cyrus,
Richard 11., Henry VI., Moses, and the
Pharaoh of the Exodus ; who or what was
William Tell; did Jean Cousin really discover
America ; and several billion other questions.
Spiritualism, which professes to be able to rake
up the ghosts of the dead past, should be able
to answer all these problems, but it seldom gets
further than rousing up a modern spectre with
a strong Scotch accent, who breathes for a
while and then goes away. And when it does
profess to arouse the wraith of a great write r
or poet or statesman he always proves to be the
same plain, drivelling idiot as the rest. It s
about time for a fresh run on Besant’s famous
spiritualistic novel, “Herr Paclus,” which
contains many valuable tips concerning the sad'
and mangy science of ghosts, and is wholesome
reading after too much of the Melton variety
of literature.
The paralysing effect of conventional religion
was never better shown than in the statement
by a Wairarapa passenger that “ many of the
poor people were praying and not holding on to
anything, so when the seas broke over the
vessel they were washed off.” Humanity learns
very slowly. As far back as we can trace its
beginnings it has been praying to a mysterious
something which it supposes to exist at the
back of natural forces, and imploring it to alter
the order of the universe to suit petty human
longings. Nothing has ever come of all the
supplications ; the gods have turned a deaf ear
to all their worshippers ; science has made it
clear that the world is governed by law, and
that breach of law is pitilessly punished ; yet
still the old, savage superstition finds vent in
vain implorings, even, as in the Wairarapa
instance, in the very jaws of death. The
crippling influence of creeds which teach a man
to pray to Heaven instead of gripping a rope is
a legacy from our prehistoric forefathers
which accumulated experience with the greatest
difficulty is extirpating.
Apropos The Bulletin’s comments on the
legal morality of Q. Attorney-General Byrnes,
a legal correspondent writes to Brisbane
Observer, pointing out that Byrnes’ law is
worse than his legal morals, and winding up :
It is better for all concerned that a solicitor should no
longer enjoy that immunity from ordinary criminal
punishment he has in many cases enjoyed. He may
receive money for investment, collect debts, sums due on
mortgage, rents, &c., for a client, and yet, unless that
client has expressly constituted him a trustee in writing,
or in writing instructed him to apply the moneys so
received or collected in a particular way, he is absolutely
outside the criminal law.
Byrnes, who has quite as much brass as law,
condescended no reply ; and the fact remains as
stated that a solicitor may embezzle his
clients’ money to any extent unless he receives
written orders not to embezzle. And, all over
Australia, clients elect lawyers to Parliament
to back up one another’s thievery. But lots of
the clients only want the chance to thieve too.
Queensland Govt, is postponing till the end of
the session the loan indispensable to prop up
its financial proteges, in the hope that a tired
Parliament will pass the Bill at once—“ and no
questions asked.” The bait Boddi.ewraith
dangles before the eyes of its supporters is a
half-dozen or so of railway lines—none of them
really very much wanted ; while all of them,
whether wanted or not, should be done without
till the Treasury pays off the existing deficit.
Of course, everybody knows that “ public
works ” will never even smell the money, which
will be simply added to the millions deposited
with the banks, while the “works” are post-
poned on one pretext or other till the crocodiles
begin to fly. But it is all taken as a matter of
course up North. People know Boodlewraith,
and Boodlewraxth knows them.

xiio Wilci-Cat Column.
The Bank of Australasia reports having made a
profit of £48,000 for the half-year—a serious falling-off
from the £60,000 realised in the previous six months —
and the div. at Die rate of 6 per cent, per annum is a
solemn thing when compared with the 35 per cent, of a
very few years ago. The Australasia’s reserve fund stands
at the same figure as in 1887. Its note circulation has
fallen off during the half-year by over £50,000, and iia
deposits by £200,000. It has also fully £600,000 less
available coin than it had at last balance, and £115.000
less in Govt, securities. Therefore, it is evident
that the present reduction in profits does not arise
through money lying idle. The Australasia is now 60
years old, and it has always been noted as a slow and
almost torpid institution. Between 1860 and 1880 it made
hardly any headway. Then it had a good time up to ’B7,
and ever since that date it has barely held its own, its
general tendency being towards a shrinkage in business.
As a beautiful example of the British conservatism with
which it is managed, £500,000 of its reserve is invested in
British consols at 2| per cent. Australian Government
debentures at 4 per cent, would yield £6OOO a year more
profit, hut the Australasia seemingly abjures mere
“kawlonial” securities.
♦ • • *
David Elder, of the N.Z. Loan and Mercantile,
suggests, as a mild and pleasing system of bank re-
arrangement, that depositors should accept 3 or 3i instead
of 4J per cent., and that the banks should put annually
1 per cent, to a sinking fund, to be invested in Govt,
securities, as a means of ultimate repayment. By this
scheme, he points out, the whole liabilities would easily
be liquidated in 50 years. Directors have been so im-
press'd in the past by the patient idiocy of the depositor
that Mr. E. and a good many others think there is nothing
extraordinary about the suggestion that he should not
only accept reduced interest, but wait half-a-century for
the principal.
» * • •
The Bank of New South Wales presents a
balance-sheet which altogether “ lays over ” those of its
two big rivals. Its net profits for the half-year are close
on £79,000, and it pays a div. at the rate of 9 per cent, per
annum, against 10 per cent, in April last. Its capital has
increased to £1,786,760, and it has made £28,815 in pre-
miums on new shares, which goes to swell the reserve
fond—said fund now amounting to £1,144,190. Also, it
has nearly £4,900,000 incash, which breaks the Australian
record up to date, and reckoning Govt, securities and
Queensland State notes, which are cash to all intents and
pmposes. its r> sources under this head are more than
£5,600,000. Altogether the B.N.S.W.’s sheet is the best
document of the kind which this depressed country has
seen for a long time.

A transaction in Irrigable Estate Co.’s shares
that came to light in a Melb. court case the other day is
first cousin to Tommy Bent’s bounty. A certain magnifi-
cent P.M.—haw!— having afi.fa. out against him for a
small debt, “ pointed out ” 100 I.E. shares to the bailiff,
and these T» f e taken for £1 by an ingenuous individual
who was advised to buy by his solicitor—although there
was a trifle of £9OO in calls owing on the shares in ques-
tion! The -Micitor also provided the purchase-money,
by ttie way. Application for transfer refused. The man
who lives upon his debts is not half smart compared with
the genius who can satisfy a writ of ft. fa. with calls to
the amount of £9OO. As to the man who bought the
shares, he looks, on paper, as if he would buy elephantiasis
if it were only offered him cheap enough.

*i • * •
Some half-dozen of the members of the late
Australasian Rights Co., determined that the opposition
crowd shall not get ahead of them, have clubbed together
and will submit a re-hash of the old co.’s bill to N.S. VY.
Parliament with the leasr. possible delay. They will pose
as genuine N.S.W. capitalists, quite distinct from the
supporters of the late co., and their only desire will be to
help a despondent country out of the slough by scatter-
ing their capital broadcast, affording employment to all
and sundry, and supplying electric-light and power at
unheard of cheap rates.

One insurance agent, canvassing Sydney for the
last two years, made not more than £ll5 commission in
all. But the recent, railway catastrophe, and the wreck
of the Wairarapa, threw 4 800 commission in his way in
one day. Big accidents bleed the companies’ noses, it is
true ; but they also pump blood into the companies’
arteries.

Mr. Barton Lodge, chief-inspector of the A. J.S.
Bank, has died in England. Mr. Lodge, whose demise
will be greatly regretted, was fairly broken down by the
strain of the financial crisis, and reached Europe in a hope-
less state.

Victoria has a merchant-squatter whose gigantic
losses in the past have puzzled the 'cutest for the last 10
years as to how he stood it. The problem will be solved
next week—the three banks who have the choicest
collection of his autographs having resolved to “put him
through.” ,
A loan for £59,000, recently floated by the Bal-
main (Sydney) Municipal Council, shows the dull and
fatuous borough fathers in a strong light. Certain old
debentures were falling due, and it was necessary to
borrow again to provide for them. Therefore, it was
decided to raise the above sum at the astounding interest
of 51 per cent, for a period of 20 years! One of the
aldermen held in reverence as a financial expert a gen-
tleman named Cohen, and the whole loan was issued to
Cohen at par, less a commission which seems to have
been given to Cohen for his trouble in taking up the
debentures. Then the same astute Cohen sold the
securities in London at a minimum of 108, and made
something like £6OOO out of the transaction. About
half-a-dozen big Australian insurance societirs, and a
dozen banks, and any number of private capitalists are
now wandering to and fro upon the face of the earth
looking for a safe investment even at a humble rate of
interest, and if tenders had been called locally the money
could have been obtained at 5 per cent.—if not at 4i—
over and over again. The inferences to be drawn from
the transaction are—(l) No municipality should borrow
through any alderman’s god, Cohen; (2) All municipal
accounts should be periodically overhauled by Govern-
ment auditors; and (3) all municipal borrowings should
be conducted under these auditors’ supervision, and not
be wholly left to dunderheaded aldermen. A tendency to
play the fool in matters of finance is the alderman’s
most conspicuous characteristic.
* » * •
Ex-Premier Shiela, at the meeting of the City of
Melbourne Bank, and again at that of the Colonial, pleaded
for amalgamation of the two institutions. If Parliament
would grant certain concessions in the way of stamp-duties,
&c., so that the work could be done cheaply, amalgamation
would mean an immediate economy of probably £22,000 a
year, which would at least be something. Then both the
old, unsavoury names could be sunk, which is also some-
thing. The banks are about the same size, they are about
equally bad, and they are so small that they would only
make one reasonable institution between them. The
main difficulty is that each crowd of shareholders is afraid
lest its own hank is a little worse than the other one, and
doesn’t want to expose its weakness—a senseless fear, for
neither of them could be much worse than the other one
just now. Shiels’ case is that union will effect a big
saving of salaries, and that one bank can’t be worse than
two, anyhow—both of which are incontrovertible pro-
positions.
* • • *
One great reason why The Bulletin wants the
State to come to the rescue of the embarrassed banks by
issuing bonds at a low rate of interest in lieu of their
deposits and then—for a consideration—carefully nursing
them back to solvency, is that the smash which seems
to be coming will leave the English banks in possession
of half, or more than half, the financial business of Aus-
tralia. And if Australia isn’t tired of grinding out divs.
for British shareholders she ought to be. Hardly any-
thing worse could happen to this country than the collapse
of the local banks, bad as most of them are and have been.
To the man with the overdraft it may seem a matter of no
importance where his taskmaster belongs to, but to the
country at large, which is already sufficiently depleted
of its currency by sending “ Horae ” interest on foreign
capital, it means a great deal.

The Ballarat (Vic.) Woollen and Worsted Co.
announces that it has made £2600 profit for the half-
year, that the last instalment of the debt of £25,000,
with which the co. commenced operations, has been
liquidated, and that, after paying for considerable addi-
tional plant, there is enough over to provide a div. of 7j
per cent, to preference shareholders. Next half-year,
there being no more mortgages to pay off, will probably
see alO per cent. div. all round. There are 240 hands
employed in the factory, and the wages paid during last
half-jear were £l2OO more than those of the preceding
six months. Which facts don’t square any too well with
the Free Raid wheeze about the decadence and hopeless
bursting-up of the protected woollen trade of Victoria.

there was also no show ; and No. 1 shaft and the underlay
excavation sunk on the reef near which Dunn got the
specimens are also apparently d-stitute of gold-bearing
stone. It is a big poor reef. Dunn s specimens were

Cashman’s, nothing more, having for Us foundation some

The Lady Shenton mine, on Menzies find, is
the most permanent-looking show of the Ninety-Mile
district. Reef about 3ft., opening by trenches several
hundred yards proving one pay-shoot 120 ft. long, another
about 50; looks like an average of 4oz. stone. AMr
cash offer was made for this mine, a month ago, by a well-
known, cautious and much-travelled English and Ameri-
can representative, whose fame as an expert is world-
wide, and whose co-operation would have been invaluable
to the field. But the Perth people immediately con-
cluded that it was too good to sell. Now it is announced
that some of the stone will average 200 oz. per ton, and
that to protect its richness shareholders are sleeping on
the reef—with a lot more Coolgardie lies.

« « « •
It is now explained, from several quarters, that
there is no cause for alarm amongst the Fortune of War
shareholders. The mine-manager tackled the big heap
left at grass when he took charge with a light heart. He,
like the shareholders, was led to believe it was rich, but
it was very poor in the centre. How very characteristic
of most heaps of quartz on new goldfields! The writer
knows a hundred like heaps in Coolgardie—the best stone
not buried or otherwise concealed for protection, but care-
fully built around the heap to be smelt at. The next
excuse is that the salt water made it difficult to catch the
fine gold. Anyhow, the crushing was a ghastly dis-
appointment.

Junction (8.H.) acknowledges a loss for half-
year to September 30 of £1921105. 9d. Of this £9Bllos.
(estimated) accrues on a previous half-year and is repre-
sented by an item of freight and charges on one of the
large parcels of ore despatched to Europe, which, for
some unexplained reason, was not allowed for in last
balance-sheet. A few such half-years would bring
about a second crisis in the history of the Junction, and
probably terminate in the permanent shutting-down of
the mine. The shareholders will not regard kindly
another call-paying period.
The “ accrued ” item is a significant commentary
on balance-sheets in general. The Junction for several
half-years has been credited with variable amounts of
profit. For the half-year preceding the last, for instance,
the profit was stated as £916 10s. The estimated £9Bllos.
probably accrues from this half-year, which is to say that
the profit shown, of close upon £lOOO, has been meta-
morphosed into a loss of £65.

The inevitable Darvell having applied for a
lease in the Bingara district, the fact is published through-
out N.S.W. This reef is unfortunately only 40ft. wide,
and prospects only' an ounce or two to the ton.
The contingency which Wyalongers most fear is
not want of water but too much of it. It is thought—
despite official opinions to the contrary—that water will
come in at the 200 ft. levels (one or two claims have
already been flooded at 150 ft.) in which case only a very-
few of the reefs will he large enough to work profitably,
and 12 months ought to see the last of them.

Bendigo mines and gold-cakes, flat, small, and
unprofitable. The deep-sinking craze has enbed right
out, after a million of money has been wasted on it. Deep
sinking on Bendigo has proved a costly failure. The new
Bendigo fad is to develops the side lines of reef. Any.
thing to save an all-round slump, but slump it must.

Struck Oil, near Mount Morgan, The Bulletin
bears, is very promising. Already the small local b Uery
is fully employed, and the one at Crocodile ; also Monk-
ton's claim looks particularly well, after the prospectors’.
Not enough deep sinking, however, has been done to
justify any startling predictions as to the future.
* * * *
An oft-quoted example (in N. Queensland) of
combined luck and pluck, is Tom Mills, the largest share-
holder in Mills United, Charters Towers. He was one of
the few who had great faith in the “deep” ground, and
although the expense incurred in sinking the Mills United
shaft gave him a severe knock financially, he never
flinched, and now has the satisfaction of drawing a regu-
lar monthly div. of 6d. per share (the capital is 300,000 £1
shares, paid-up to 155.) and seeing the stock quoted at 265.
He confines his mining speculations to Queensland.
* * * *
Dear Wild Cat, — “S.S.” turns on his wrath
about Melb. Mining Exhibition. You had already said it
was not a spectacular success, and that covered the
ground. Despite “ 5.5.,” one or two of the obji ots of the
exhibition were realised. The practical attention of
brainy men was invited to the tremendous tailings
question, and one or two good notions were given decent
publicity. At least two good portable pulverisers were
on view. There were several excellent germs of good
gold savers, and at least one success. It would have
saved a mass of money to Westralian prospectors passing
through if just such a circumscribed show had been open
during the big rush.—F.
* ♦ • •
Dear Bulletin,- Talking of gold-saving appli-
ances, I remember, as a small boy, witnessing a competi-
tion between two machines at Bendigo, the relative
proprietors of which were a Cousin Jack and a Milesian.
Twenty grains of gold were put into two loads of ordinary
vegetable loam, ten into each. The loam wouldn’t go a
color to the acre. The Irishman got BJgrs. out of his
load, the Cousin Jack 14grs. out of his ; and yet the Irish-
man took the cake. I thought this outrageously unfair
at the time, but I have since learned to take tests with a
pinch of salt.—C.M.G.
• * • •
Printed to encourage correspondence on the
part of interested persons :
Dear Bulletin,— Having read your comments on Mr.
O’Sullivan’s remarks re poor reefs in N.S.W. and Vic., let
me give you a few particulars of one on the Etheridge
(Q.)_viz., the Marquis Hill, This hill has a reef from one
end to the other lying very flat, so that it can be quarried
freely, and there are thousands of tons of surface from
which we are now crushing at a five-head mill of heavy
stamps, with the aid of a good water-supply. The crush-
ing stuff costs us Is. 3d. per ton to land at the mill.
Total expenses (mill, carting and raising) under £5O per
week —from which we average 40oz. per week, worth £3 per
oz., making an average of 4dwts. per ton for 200 tons. I
think this beats the record for five head. The crushing
stuff consists of loose dirt and pebbly quartz. As soon as
we can we mean to erect another 10-head, as there is any
quantity of stuff. The show is owned by four men, all
working shareholders. Any information re the Otis or
other crushers from mines which have made extended
practical tests will be welcome. We want to know if they
will put through the quantity stated, and if the wear and
tear is very great.—Yours truly. Shareholder, Marquis
Hill, Etheridge (N.Q.), Oct. 10. [2bo tons a week with
five head it tall.—Ed. B.]
• • * •
Messrs. Noyes Bros., Melb., representing Fred.
Krupp, of Essen, write that the Golden Bar mine, W.A.,
is not ordering the Otis crusher, but Krupp’s patent ball-
mill, No. 5; also, that at present 700 Krupp ball-mills
are at work.

N.S.W. Speaker Joseph Abbott tells how
he inspected a convent school in the Darling dl
trict. and was mistaken by one of the little girl
pupils for St. Joseph. The story seems probable
enough ; there is a pale and saintly aspect about
Joe Abbott as he looks out from behind the
ambush of his whiskers, which is calculated to
deceive any small girl.
Yet another big Sydney paper has decided
to cease hand-setting; it was announced to the
HEBAtD compositors last week that machines
would be erected forthwith. The machine is not
identical with that in use at Sydney D.T, office,
but is one of English make, much used in London,
and which sets, instead of casting, type. Sydney
Evening News has also apparently begun to use
machines.
See the picture of the Crucifixion (photo,
from the immortal Russian painter, Gay) in the
current Review of Reviews. It is the most aw-
fully impressive thing ever committed to paper.
When you have ceased staring at it, which will be
in, say, half-an-hour, you will say to yourself :
“The Rev. John M‘Neill, popular Christian evan-
gelist, stays when in Sydney at the gilt-edged
Australia Hotel.”
The Wairarapa wreck has broken the spell
of the “ unlucky T ” which has for years been of
such ill-omen to the vessels of the Union S.S. Co.
Such of the Co.’s boats as have met with disaster
attended by loss of life have almost invariably had
names commencing with “T”—Tararua, Taiaroa,
Taupo, Taranaki. If The Bulletin’s memory
is not at fault the Wairarapa is the first “ W ” to
come to grief. The Wairarapa, by the way, was
pronounced by Archibald Forbes to be the best
steamer in which he had ever travelled.
Perhaps the most pertinent query of all
the host which have followed the recent Sydney
spook-eruption is “What do spooks live on?” If
they can eat chocolate-creams they must have a
stomach to put them in, and then what else do
they put in their stomachs when chocolate-creams
are “off?” This question, however, was answered
once and for all in the heyday of spiritualism 40
years ago—when spirits first, at Home’s command,
arose from out the azure main. Spooks cannot
carry weight through the blue ethereal, and their
sole sustenance, apart from casual confectionery,
is that subtly invigorating compound, the essence
of good spirits, Marshall’s Tonic Nervine. Sold
only by Marshall Bros., Park-street, Sydney, and
Pitt and Regent streets, Redfern.

“The New Boy,” which opened the
comedy-season at Sydney Lyceum, would have
been more interesting if “Vice Versa” had never
been written, and if various other things that
were hadn’t been. As it is, it loses a good deal of
its thrill by comparison. “ The New Boy,” like
the very old and weary boy in Anstey’s yarn, is a
man forced by a ghastly combination of circum-
stances to go to school again, and wrestle with
venerable Latin roots, and tackle weird arith-
metical conundrums concerning llflbs. of cheese
at 7|d. per lb. The principal difference between
the two comedies lies in the fact that Anstey’s
man is transformed into a boy through fooling
with a magic relic, while the other man relies on
the fact that he is short and hairless, and wears
knickerbockers. The complications which drive
him into a select academy are amusing, but not
especially convincing; and the way he is pounded
by Bullock, the senior lout, when he gets there,
and how the schoolroom tea disagrees with him,
and how he is sent out to steal green apples, and how
his wife professes to be his mother, and how, after
nearly being caned on several thrilling occasions,
a magistrate at last orders him to be birched, and
he explains everything to avoid the indignity, are
all told at length in three acts. Then the school-
master proceeds to cane Bullock, the senior lout,
and the show ought to end amid the howls of that
large, coarse youth coming from somewhere in the
back premises, but this effect is unfortunately left
out.
* * *
Mr. Roberts, as the 3Sew Boy, is intelli-
gent and painstaking, and looks sufficiently
feasible in his knickerbockers, but he manifests no
striking brilliance or originality. He is a plain
boy, and that is about all. A certain fascinating
swindler, played by Mr. Clifton Alderson, is the
character which keeps “The New Boy” afloat.
This wild-eyed person is the only stage-swindler
on record whose yellow hair stands straight out
from his head in |all directions, and he has many
good points which can’t be enumerated here for
want of space. Regarded as a well-dressed and
persuasive groveller, he takes a very high place,
and his abilities ought to keep the play afloat for
some time.
• * *
Miss Jenny Watt-Tanner is the next best
feature of the comedy, and the other five or
six members of the co. are about equal in merit,
save perhaps Eugene Mayeur, who is rather more
equal than the rest. There is no tail to the co.,
and with a little more head it would get on very
well. By some gloomy miscalculation “The New
Boy ” is preceded by a ghoulful shriek, in one act,
entitled “To Call Her Mine.” There is a
thunderstorm in it, and a stormy sea howling
against the rocks, and a drowning sailor washing
to and fro along with the crab and the corpse-fed
eel, and Clifton Alderson, who hates the drowning
sailor, laughs a Horrid Laugh in his cottage, and
sees a dripping ghost. Then he goes out and
saves the drowning sailor and is drowned himself,
and somebody comes in and states that they are
bringing along his dank remains on a board. Mr.
Alderson is a good comedian, but, regarded as a
melodramatic person who sees ghosts, he is a very
solemn institution, and the way he rolls his tragic
sentences along, as if they were chariots rolling
over a stony road, is painful to listen to.
* # •
G. Rignold still takes up arms against a
sea of troubles at Sydney Majesty’s, where
“Siberia” makes reasonable headway. The
moving spectacle of Miss Athena Claudius start-
ing with her hair down for her frozen place of
exile continues to take sufficiently well for all
practical purposes. The effect is heightened later
on, when she is seen, with her hair down, wearing
the yoke of the oppressor, with a water-bucket
slung at each end of it; and afterwards, when
she escapes from her persecutors, with her hair
down, the effect is eminently pleasing. Finally,
she is seen embarking for the happy shores of
England, where, presumably, she lives happily
with her hair down for ever afterwards, while the
wicked but beautiful Governor, Scot Inglis, _ is
left behind in chains. G. Rignold accompanies
her in her flight, and would doubtless fly with his
hair down also, only it is cut too short to allow of
such a thing. Taking it all round, “Siberia” is
a somewhat hairy play, and a clean shave all
round would produce a startling change, especially
m the case of a certain cheerful soldier, whose
countenance is like two eyes looking out of a wool-
bale.
• # #
Messrs, Ashton and Woods have leased
Sydney Royal, and open on Friday (9th) with
that patriotic British drama, “ Hands across^ the
Sea.” Said drama contains more burning allusions
to the Union Jack, and its tendency to “ wave ”
on the slightest provocation, and the fact that
wherever it waves freedom immediately ensues,
than any other production of the same size on the
modern stage. Therefore all persons who like to
be prodded into a patriotic frenzy by hearing
about the Union Jack will do well to be on hand.

It is announced, be on view in Sydney for some
days longer. There were thoughts of removing
her, but the good business done has decided the
proprietary to keep their painted siren on exhi-
bition till further orders.
* * * *
At Melb. Princess’s they mustered a
Derby Night crowd of old-time proportions, in
honour of the comic opera co.’s reappearance.
“Ma Mie Rosette” was a more popular event
than ever, and when Nellie Stewart, who had
been generally admired in the paddock a few
hours earlier, made her supreme effort to the
familiar tune of “I was Dreaming,” she just
showed her ankles to the field and romped home
without turning a hair of her flaxen wig. “ Paul
Jones” was to alleviate the inevitable pangs of
Cup regret, and “Mascotte ” is the bill for Thurs-
day and Friday. On Saturday, an impoverished
racing public will invest their losings in “Mile.
Nitouche ” and the airy tableaux vivants.
* * * *
Maggie Moore, at Melb. Royal, was another
booming incident of Saturday. The experiment
of M. M. and her dramatic forces in “Little Jack
Sheppard ” appeared risky before the curtain rose;
yet the result proved the wide gulf between
“ Struck Oil ” and Gaiety burlesque is negotiable
by a nimble band of performers when a clever
woman shows the way. Apart from a few fail-
ings in the orchestra and some rugged points in
the chorus-singing, “Little Jack Sheppard” was
a success on its first night. With practice, this
entertainment, at popular prices, should be able
to challenge all-round comparison with memories
of the past and make a “draw” for certain.
Maggie Moore is not physically little, but
otherwise she plays the name-part for its
full value. H. R. Roberts makes a spry Jonathan
Wilde, and his sister is a bright, fresh young
Winifred. Miss Conroy is a capital variety of
Thames Darrell, also Miss Hardy and other
damsels cast for minor characters are distinct im-
provements upon their predecessors. Dick
Stewart and Constance Deorwyn adapt them-
selves to new circumstances, old Daniels plays
Mendez as before, and the spectacle is well
managed in all particulars. Oily Deering’s Blue-
skin might be a good deal better. On the other
hand, a much less efficient comedian might have
been chosen for the ungrateful task of imper-
sonating Royce in his L°«*--rpmembered character.
** * *
“Charley’s Aunt,” at the Opera House,
was a fact not generally understood on Saturday,
judging from the awful scarcity of attendance.
Melbourne has already seen a good deal of that
riotous female from the land of nuts, nevertheless
she can’t have exhausted all her resources in the
city. Before the present week is ended “ Charley’s
Aunt ” ought to make her presence felt in a man-
ner satisfactory to the management. She happens
to be struggling against special difficulties, for the
very name of Opera House was superseded by the
term “Alhambra ” long ago, and thus it is a ease
of a show coming straight from a “farewell”
season at one theatre (the Princess’) to another
theatre which had lately been lost behind an alias.
