The Bulletin is earnestly desired to men-
tion that a concert will take place at the Masonic
Hall, Melbourne, on Tuesday, the 13th inst., in
aid of a good cause. The cause in question is the
Consumptive Sanatorium of Echuca, which sorely
needs funds. Consumption is unhappily abundant,
but cash becomes tighter every day. This concert
appeals to healthy citizens, who will be admitted
free and expected to put a trifle in the plate,

At Poverty Point.
Arthur Dacre, who signed an agreement for self and
wife (Amy Roselle) for an Australian season, and actually
sold up his home, besides securing several good
plays and buying expensive dresses, finds himself in
the soup, writes The Bulletin’s London correspondent.
When the full facts come out, the Dacres will be much
sympathised with. They were offered good engagements
for South Africa, which they could not, under the circs.,
accept.
Seagar, the new Charley’s Aunt, is a very diminutive
mummer, and looks wonderfully like a sparrow flirting
about the stage in a dress-suit. He has none of Thornton’s
unction, and is a closer imitator of Penley, but imitation
is not always the successfullest form of flattery.

“ Nothing succeeds like success.’’ No sooner (remarks
The Bulletin’s London correspondent) did Beerbohm
Tree hear of the hit made by “ The Fatal Card ” than he
telegraphed for Haddon Chambers to go to him at Edin-
burgh to discuss the scenario of the new play which had
been written for him ; doubtless it will be selected to
follow “A Bunch of Violets” at the Haymarket. Had
not the critics gushed about “The Fatal Card,” Chambers
would have waited long ere he saw his name again upon
the play-bills of that theatre.
The Argos account of Abel and Rlaer’s circus, on the
opening Melb. night, described how the wheel of the
monkeys’ coach came off and the monkeys descended and
replaced the wheel. As a matter of fact the monkey who
should have done this got involved in a quarrel with
one of the spectators sitting near the ring, and the trainer
had to do the coach repairs himself. But the clever
simians have made a big hit.

Seagar, the new Charley’s Aunt, talks very like that
johnnie-chappie in “Morocco Bound.” Who was the
original seed-idiot of this sort, and what made him talk
like that, and where in England do they teach people to
talk “chaw-chaw?” By the way, it isn’t absolutely
necessary to be reared in England to talk “chaw-chaw.”
The eyeglassed professional youth—at least he once was a
youth—who when he is talking “champs” more than
any other person ever heard on land or sea, is the son of
an Australian judge, who is himself an Australian; and
“ Jawge,” who apart from—or, if you like, even includ-
ing, the “ chaw-chaw ” —is an immensely good fellow, is
understood never to have been out of Australia in all his
life.

Of the two Melb. pantomimes the Royal has the
prettier and shapelier, and the Princess's the older and
more experienced chorus and ballet girls. The former
receive 15s. and the latter 255. per week.
Several stage-struck society girls who were engaged at
an Australian metropolitan theatre recently simply to
“go on" in a burlesque, failed to answer the "call one
morning, and, when asked for an explanation, asserted
that the call-board notice was: “Chorus, Ballet and
Extras, 10.30;” and, as they considered themselves
“ Principals,” they had not obeyed the order. Next day
the assistant stage-manager posted a “call” thus:
“ Chorus, Ballet and Society Young Ladies.” That had
the desired effect, but the society young ladies became
very indignant, considered it a great insult, and appealed
to the manager, who dismissed the A.S.M.
The Sapio-Urso Co. travelled to M.L. per Wairarapa,
but disliked her so much during the first trip that they
lost a week in Auckland rather than continue the coast
voyage in her.
McNicol Turner, the self-advertising young man who
secretaries Melb. Lyric Club and stars himself on its pro-
grammes, is, like the Cunninghams, a prominent oars-
man, being a member of that toney institution, the Mel-
bourne U.C., a club which has distinguished itself by
being the most superior in its ideas and the most inferior
in its rowing of any on the Yarra. The Melbourne is
about the only Vic. rowing club that still clings to
the ancient custom of excluding manual-workers.
Maddocks, the diminutive tenor, a son of the

by Princes Bridge.

The most striking feature of Melb.
Cathedral service at Dean Macartney’s funeral
was the advanced age of most of the diocesan
clergy. Out of the 70 or so present only about 15
per cent, seemed under 45. Of the remainder
many were over 60—the age at which the local
legislature declares men to be worthless. Once a
clergyman gets a good billet he sticks like wax,
and smiles blandly at the congregation’s strongest
hints. The only way to get rid of the aged
incubus is to stop his screw. Then he goes
instanter, calling on the Lord to curse you.
More S.M. Herald slobber :
The death of the venerable Sir Alfred Stephen, who for
eo long a period had occupied moat important offices in
the State, and who by his high character and amiability,
added to his talents, was equally admired, respected and
loved by the people of all ranks ; and as he was honored
in life, the regret has been general at his death, and it
has been felt that so long and useful a life was fittingly
closed by the painless, peaceful manner in which the end
came. All that respectful, genuine, &c.
The anguish of this eloquent scribe, it will be
perceived, was so poignant that he forgot to finish
his sentence. Grief paralysed his grammar !
A few items from the evidence re Waira-
rapa wreck last week, on Great Barrier Island,
M. whereby over 115 lives were lost: “The
primary cause was the vessel going at a high rate
of speed through a dense fog, and striking on a
rock some 20 miles out of her proper course. . .
Captain MTntosh had been remarkably free from
accidents, and had the reputation of being a sin-
gularly careful officer. . . The precautions
specified in the regulations for vessels at sea dur-
ing thick weather were not observed. The engines
were not slowed down, the lead was not used, and
the foghorn was not blown. . . Captain
MTntosh was a splendid navigator, a grand dis-
ciplinarian, and a solid man.” And if the un-
fortunate captain, who was amongst the drowned,
had missed that disaster by 20 yards, he would be
all these things still. A splendid seaman is evi-
dently a man who takes desperate risks and has
the luck to come out right; and a bad seaman is
the same man when his luck leaves him, and the
pitcher goes to the well once too often.
Editor Bulletin. Along the eastern
shore of Darling Harbor, Sydney, are a number of
pieces of land never alienated by the State, which
have been jumped by, and are in the possession of,
certain enterprising people. Some of this land
has even been brought under the Real Property
Act—illegally, of course, as possession gives no
right against the State. These lands are valued
by Mr. Engineer Darley at .£l5O (one hundred
and fifty pounds) per foot frontage. The facts are
known to both the Lands and Works Depart-
ments ; also to the members of the late Public
Works Committee and the late Minister for Lands
and Works. Not the slightest attempt is being
made to put the State into possession of these
lands and the considerable revenue derived from
them, and, as usual, in the fulness of time the
country will purchase its own property at three
times its then value from the people who have
grabbed it, or their assigns. It was openly said at
the time—and never, I believe, denied —that Darling
Island, to resume which £90,000 was paid, had
never been alienated, but merely jumped, like the
lands just mentioned. Isn’t it nearly time that
N. gave up the sorry farce of making believe
that Government is run in the interests of the
people, and acknowledged What is pretty palpable,
anyhow—that N.S.W. institutions merely exist
for the benefit of land-grab? All the facts in con-
nection with this matter may be found in the
schedule to the “Return respecting proposed
reclamation of Darling Harbor,” printed June 8,
1894.— Yours, Charles Brown.
“My hands are clean—and empty.”—
Kossuth.
The Vic. Artists’ Society is holding its
annual exhibition of pictures under specially
depressing circumstances. In the first place, the
collection of new works is so small that the broad
tracts of gallery at the Society’s disposal are
mostly decorated with old and awful paintings,
contributed by the members to a mysterious art-
union which was inaugurated six months ago, and
threatens to drag much ere reaching an issue. In
the second place, the dreary drip of desultory
visitors into this commodious building denotes
that public belief in Victorian art has been shat-
tered by its high priests. Apart from personal
friends 'of exhibitors, the daily pilgrims to the
shilling show at Albert-street might be counted,
perhaps, on the fingers of the doorkeeper, whose
assumption of briskness is one of the most artistic
things on the premises.
Eighty-four frames include the whole annual
exhibition of paintings in oil. Taken altogether,
they are considerably below the average merit of
last year’s display, and the trail of Victorian art
is over most of them. Even a tried and trusty
performer like J. Mather seems to be degenerating
into unnaturalism, as witness his chromo-like
“Evening, Healesville.” Near it hangs “ Moon-
rise,” a much-belauded work by D. Davies. A
beautifully round and bright moon rises in a blue-
gray sky, upon a sagey-greeny desert, the land-
scape being relieved by faint reflections of crimson
glory from a sun which has lately set. There is a
pretentiously gloomy school of art that threatens
to exterminate all other schools in Victoria, thanks
to the critics who praise its dyspeptic results.
Fifteen or twenty efforts at reproducing Nature in
her ugliest moods must have been incited by a
combination of tough meat, bad cookery and
strong tea. Portraits of “ Mile. M. L.” and Miss
Adey Brunei, by Tudor St. G. Tucker, are clever,
wholesome views of pretty women effectively dis-
played, though it must be admitted that Miss
Brunei’s face on the canvas is not a striking like-
ness of the original. J. C. Waite’s portrait of
Winter Irving, M.L.0., commands admiration for
the artist, and the children of Dr. Springthorpe,
by E. P. Fox, make a satisfactory picture.
Artists can’t afford to be eccentric in treating such
subjects.
It is when he gets under the broad canopy of
Heaven that the Victorian genius indulge* his
taste for originality. A bogus landscape impres-
sion, veiled by a weird artificial mist, must have
appeared to some unhappy genius in a vision years
ago, whereupon he painted an impossible picture,
from which the modern caricatures of earth,
foliage, sky, air and water have emanated. The
proportion of misty smudges and crazy-colored
bush-scenes in these exhibitions grows bigger
year by year. Artists get drawn into the
vortex of impossibilities and lost before they
know where they are. Miss J., Sutherland
painted pleasant little suggestions of the Creator’s
handiwork twelve months ago, but now she
goes in for Victorian art-tints, and produces
foliage that must have been dyed in a double
extract of orange-peel. Walter Withers depicts a
chimney-pot of the same color, so perhaps it has
been specially manufactured for the society. A
good man in John Ford Paterson has seemingly
sacrificed his early principles. His formidable
picture, “ Evening,” is sky for the most part-dull,
impenetrable sky that conveys littlesense of atmos-
phere. Many cheerful panels are to be found smiling
amidst the bile and misery of the landscape
studies. Bits of gorse, clusters of chrysanthe-
mums, and other floral growths are, in most cases,
skilfully and kindly dealt with on wood. Also,
there are several bunches of grapes, but the mono-
tony of grapes is growing intolerable.
Neuralgia
is the first, surest, and most reliable sign of nerve
decay; it is a symptom provided by nature to
warn the sufferer of approaching weakness and
distress. Though in most cases the pain is purely
local, the disease is constitutional; and local reme-
dies such as liniments, oils, and menthols only give
temporary relief, and do not do the slightest per-
manent good, for they only act by deadening the
sensibility of the affected nerve. The remedy, to
be of any lasting benefit, must be tissue-forming
and nerve-strengthening, and must have general
invigorating properties ; it must also contain the
material to restore the lost tissue to the impover-
ished nerves, and no remedy except Clements
Tonic possesses these wonderful powers. Clements
Tonic has been used in thousands of cases by
young and old of both sexes for neuralgia, sciatica,
and other nervous complaints, and we have never
heard of a case where it has failed to give
quick and prompt relief. Read of the case of
Mrs. T. Watts, Woonona, N.S.W., who writes on
March 23, 1893 :—I have suffered severely from
indigestion for the last three or four years. I was
unable to sleep, and had a continual languid feel-
ing, loss of appetite, severe pains in the chest and
stomach, and was half of my time unable to per-
form my household duties with any degree of
pleasure. I had the advice and medicine of
several doctors, but without receiving any benefit.
Hearing Clements Tonic so highly spoken of for
the above complaint, I resolved to give it a trial.
I felt myself improving from the very first dose,
and am happy to say that before I had finished
the first bottle all disagreeable symptoms had dis-
appeared, and I felt better than I had done for
years. I have never had a return of the old com-
plaint since. I recommend it on all occasions.—l
remain, yours gratefully, Mrs. T. Watts, Woo-
nona, N.S.W.

Personal Items.
Two out of the three Yic. judges las^
deceased left their widows “ well provided* for.
Both are victims of the financial smash.
At Mount Barker (S.A.) local court, Oct.
23, Mrs. Webb, 101 years old, was plaintiff in a
breach of agreement suit, which she won.
Premier Kingston now works hard at his
private law-practice. For a year after acceding
to power he never set foot in his private office, but
he is growing wise in his generation.
Rogers, a new Yic. M.P., spoke of Speaker
Berry’s appearance without a wig as “the only
gleam of sunshine ” he had seen in the House.
But really Berry is not nearly so bald as all that.
Two ex-M’s.L.A., rejected at last N.S.W.
elections, are endeavouring to forget their defeat
in the throes of historical composition. _ Both are
engaged on what they hope to call histories of
Australia.
A Vic. farmer who lately died left his
property to his wife, on condition that she
remains a widow. If she marries again the will
directs that she be allowed to “ take her husband
and one cow.”
Governor Kinlore’s mother draws so large
an annuity from the paternal estate that there is
nothing left for him. That is the chief reason
why he accepts .£5OOO a year and expatriation as
Governor of S.A.
Col. Warner, Commandant of Tasmanian
forces, has resigned that billet and accepted the
post of Governor of Hobart Gaol. This was told
to General Hutton lately as a new reading of the
sword and ploughshare adage.
Some admirers of an ex-M.L.A., badly
beaten at a recent general election, subscribed
nearly .£3OO as balm to his wounded feelings. On
the very night that he got the money the ex-M.P.
played cards and lost every shilling.
How does Cardinal Moran reconcile his
attitude against liquor when heading a children’s
total abstinence league, with the presumably per-
mitted use of his name, by wine-merchant Fallon,
as one of his most influential patrons?

Mannington Caffyn has taken consulting-
rooms in Fleet-street, London, in the well-known
house which was one of Cardinal Wolsey’s palaces,
and which is now occupied by a hair-dresser. He
intends to practise his profession diligently.

Jennie Swift, the good-tempered Aus-
tralian aboriginal who lectured through England
a dozen or so years ago, died the other day at
Warangesda. Jennie, like certain Australian
politicians, endeavoured in her latter days to
fortify most of her actions with British prece-
dents.

The large Stephen family,
though located in Sydney for three-
quarters of a century, is widely and
strangely scattered in death. The
Australian head, John Stephen,
early puisne Judge of N.S. Wales
(Supreme Court a pensioner), and Lis
wife rest in a water-filled vault in the
old Sandhills Cemetery (Belmore-
road, Sydney). Other members of the
family are there also, notably another
John Stephen, father of Melb.
Attorney Frank Stephen. Alfred
Stephen, recently dead, has beep
taken to Randwick, where lie his
second wife, her mother and a
daughter. A sister of Alfred is buried
at Yass, and bis son Consett lies in
an English grave. Another son, the
Canon, finds his last home at Rook-
wood. A third son, Edward, is in
Waverley Cemetery. Alfred’s first
wife is buried in Hobart. His
brother, George Milner, is interred
in Melb. General Cemetery, and
Milner’s son, Harold (ex-M.P.), is in
Rookwood. Other minor members of
the family repose at various points
of the compass. Alfred Stephen
makes the second N.S.W. Chief Jus-
tice interred at Randwick, James
Martin being the other.
Yic. Labor-party has a pronouncedly pious
bias, and most of its members are amateur cushion-
punchers. John Hancock can sing, without a
book, any hymns that come along ; Gray is a local
preacher ; Trenwith, Winter, and Prendergast are
prominent church-goers.
Dear Bulletin, —Not many weeks ago
Sydney newspapers recorded that Altred Stephen
had received birthday felicitations from scores of
people on reaching his 92nd anniversary. Some
papers gave lists, but, somehow or other, the fol-
lowing birthday-card (a similar one to which had
been sent annually for years to the ex-Judge) was
not mentioned; —
The spirit of Mary Brownlow congratulates you on
having reached your 92nd birthday anniversary, and
awaits you.
—D.

The Czar Alexander 111. was a big mar
and a mighty monarch. He obscured half the
world’s-eye, so to speak. Yet, see what a mere
pin-hole his departure has left in the cosmic
bladder ! Then, go, thou Swelledhead, and look
at your own shadow sideways.

Every visitor to Melb. Public Library
doesn’t go there to sleep. Some go to read—also
to mark. There is in the Barry Hall a copy of
Anthony Trollope’s book on Australia, over the
pages of which pencilled comments like these are
scattered: “Hear! Hear!” “Good iron!”
“ Alas, too true !” and, in one instance, “ You are
a liar, Anthony ! ”
Henniker Heaton’s improvement as a
writer should be an encouragement to young men
ambitious to become great. Who that reads his
wonderfully well-written article in last Pall Mall
Magazine, and others on the subject of Divorce,
can credit that they were penned by the man who
used to contribute such strangely-composed notes
of travel to the Evening Noose?
The inference loosely drawn from Mrs.
Needle’s having gained a pound and a-half in
weight during her incarceration is that she was a
woman of settled diabolic purpose, unaffected
either by fear or repentance. The much more
natural inference is that her diet and sleep and
exercise were more regular in gaol than when she
was free. Almost all the N.S.W. bank prisoners
have gained in weight—and those who were
sickliest when outside have gained the most.

An Adelaide paper tells how Yilleneuve
Smith, the S.A. lawyer, was recently opposed, in
a case, by one of the smallest advocates at the
local bar. He made a somewhat rash assertion on
a legal point, and the Judge remarked doubtfully
that he would like to hear what Smith’s learned
friend had to say on the point. “My learned
friend,” murmured Smith, with the air of a man
awaking from a dream, and, putting up his eye-
glass, he looked anxiously round the Court in all
directions. Then suddenly his eye rested on the
crown of his small opponent’s head, and he bent
down to assure himself that it was genuine. “ Oh,
there you are!” he said, joyfully ;“ I was afraid
I had lost you,” and he went on with his argu-
ment, leaving his enemy too muoh broken up to
answer.

A Sydney newspaper-man who attempted
to express himself caustically, in mixed company,
on the expenditure of .£4BO Treasury funds in
pioknicking Mackenzie-Bowell, of Canada, was
promptly pulled up by a prominent politician :
“You shut up ! You sat at the table and ate and
drank your whack of the swindle. We have
enough to do to fight the dogs that were kept in
the street.”
The great episode in the career of the
late Colonel Smith, M.P., of Ballarat (Vic.), was
the Financial Statement which he delivered in
1879. Poor Smith, who was as innocent of figures
as a child, quite forgot about the interest on the
public debt, and announced a glorious surplus of
about .£1,000,000. He was never allowed to
deliver another Financial Statement, and nobody
regarded him as a serious politician thereafter.
Evangelist M‘Neill—address, Hotel Aus-
tralia, where they chase you with a plate all day
—has a six-year-old who, the other day, asked his
pa “if ever be worked on the railway.” “ Yes,”
said the holy man. “And were you a porter?”
“Yes.” “ And did you wear rough clothes like
they do now?” “Yes.” “But that,” remarked
the bright boy, “ was before you were a gentle-
man.” Even the boy noticed it, that the Bible
paid better than the railway.
Melbourne papers continue to dig up
letters of Otto Juncken’s bearing on the psycho-
logical aspect of the threaded Mrs. Needle. In
one the young man philosophically finds much
comfort in the turn of events from reflecting that
had the truth remained unknown he would, in all
probability, have married his Martha and been
arsenicked by her in turn when she was seized by
“ that tired feeling.” A reflection like this would
reconcile a man to a much heavier loss.
Australia’s darkest-browed and most scowl-
ing- visaged legislative pietist, teetotaller, and
Orangeman has one redeeming virtue ; he is an
accomplished and inveterate card-player, and
always carries a pack about with him, to the
horror of his constituents. But he was not always
a euchre-enthusiast. When he kept a book-shop
a raw Highland digger once looked in and asked
him for a pack of cards. The future member was
wildly indignant. “Do you think,” he asked,
“I’d keep the devil’s prayer-books in my shop?”
The miner retired and told his mates, who, in
revenge, fairly pestered the saintly bookman with
enquiries for cards, of which at last, seeing money
in the business, the man of heavy eyebrows bought
a big stock cheap. But after that bookseller’s
Fall no one asked for cards, and it is surmised by
the scoffers that economical reasons prompted him
to learn the game himself rather than let the cards
go to waste !
Swilliam Clarke was really a study at the
meeting of the Colonial Bank (Melb.), at which
Shiels told the banking people generally that they
would either have to amalgamate or burst. The
Bart, labors under a fatal inability to keep awake
while any speechmaking is going on; he j ust
dozes off till it is finished, and is then nudged by
some friendly person. He seemed to be under the
impression that Shiels had been making the most
flattering and complimentary remarks, and smiled
gratefully when the jeremiad came to an end.
The gilded N.S.W. squatter
Rand, lately deceased, was an nn.
sociable hunks. One day his bank-
manager, at the nearest township,
having heard that a pet portion
of R ind’s run was going to l«
free-selected, thought to please
the old man by riding over to the
station, in company with a friend
and giving the big squats
warning. “Well, what do you
want ? ” grumpily asked Rand,
The manager imparted his news.
“ L“t him take what he likes and
In hanged to him,” surlily quoth
tb? old fellow, without a word
of thanks. “ What shall we do
with onr horses?” enquired the
bank man. “Take ’em round to
the shearers’ hut,” which they
did, and returning yarned with
Rand for a short time, when the
tea-hell rang. Then the old fellow,
with a gruff, “So long!” retired,
leaving his hungry visitors to take
their long ride home.
Col. Softgoods Sargood was
Vic-’s first Minister of Defence,
and in ’B7, when the cable was
interrupted, created a genuine
local war-scare. The sagacious
ragman saw in the cable trouble
a dark design of the enemy, who,
Sargood intuitively decided, was
the treacherous Russian. It was
Sunday, and Sargood, who is sanc-
timonious, had a passing twinge
of conscience, but heroically sup-
pressing it, by ’phone and ’graph
he ordered the fleet, reserves and
militia, to the Heads. They went,
with smiles upon their lips and
lead upon their hearts, to await
the foe. But the cable got going
again—it had merely become dis-
insulated or something, and had
not been tampered with by the
Muscovite at all. The warriors
returned to town and were
greeted with painful signs of
derision. But it is all forgotten
now, and Sargood is ready to do
the same again when he gets an
excuse.
A new story of the late Mayor Smith's
large feet. A Ballarat East mine, the Sulieman
Pasha, probably, was wanting a State-grant badly,
and desired the Mayor to back the local member
in rolling the little log. William Collard S. agreed
to honor the mine with an inspection, and went
below. Whilst he was prospecting the drives
something went wrong with the cage, which
jambed on the skids. Then the braceman was
heard shrieking to the engine driver : “Stopher,
Bill! Stop her, for the love of Heaven! The
adjective Major’s blanky feet have caught in the
crimson shaft!”
Graham Berry revives old and respectable
memories of the Vie. Speakership by appearing in
a plain black silk gown. ’Tis a meek suggestion
that the 200-guinea imported robe of Matthew
Davies and the cheaper, but gaudier, garment of
Tom Bent were mere boom-time excrescences upon
political life. The solid silver gilt Daviesiau mace
came to a bad end, and the surroundings of the
new Speaker are such as ruled in the Peter Lalor
period, when an easy billet was reckoned a tit
reward for hard service, and the first commoner in
the land had done something to entitle him to a
marble statue after his decease. Berry, when one
comes to think about it, seems positively the only
man in Victoria with a ghost of a claim upon
public gratitude.

POLITICAL
PONTS
Deakin, on dit, has an ambition to shine
in English politics.
A severe homily on the capacity of the
present N.S.W. Parliamentary Labor-party may
he founded on the smallness and incoherent
character of its contributions to the discussion of
the new Land Bill.
Dibbs and Parkes, according to the former,
haven’t spoken to each other for three years.
“We pass without recognition.” Good. It gives
the country a chance.
The newly-elected Messrs. Millen, Affleck,
and Ashton are already reckoned the champion
bores of N.S.W. Assembly, and the fluent vague-
ness of Atkinson Price is the terror of the Han-
sard gallery.
Set the lawyer to reform law, the doctor
physio, and Rosebery the Lords. But, says
Madame de Stael : “ No great revolution was
ever accomplished without the help of a section of
the aristocracy.”
Ex-Premier Patterson (Vic.) bids hotly for
the teetotal vote. Premier Turner, as attorney
Lyons’ partner, does most of ttie hotels’ legal busi-
ness, and has the Native vote, the Civil Service
vote, and Liquor vote.
I he ten members of the Labor-party and
Erooker, the representative of the big manufac-
turing suburb, were the only opponents of the
resolution in favour of Interprovineial Freetrade,
passed in S.A. Assembly last month,
Williams, Vic. Minister for Railways,
favors using horses instead of steam-engines on
non-paying lines. Glad news for the Melb. under-
takers, who have hitherto found it difficult to
dispose of horses worn out by hearse-work.
Apropos certain unsatisfactory decisions
of the N.S. Wales Elections and Qualifications
Committee : Why should that Committee be cotyi-
posed of politicians, necessarily partisans, and in
other respects Unfitted for an office above all
things judicial ?
Ex-Premier Dibbs longs for the days of
two parties only. “He has the old-fashioned
idea that Parliament could be carried on very
well by two parties, and that any more only
hamper the usefulness of the Government.” No
wonder. When there was practically but one
party, Parkes was Emperor of Botany Bay. The
two-party system clipped his wings, and the three-
party system burst him up. Then it burst Dibbs.
Presently it will burst Reid.
Victoria sees another deficit of £650,000
looming at the end of the financial year. Premier
Turner proposes, as a remedy, to raise £350,000
by a tax on unimproved land values, £25,000 by
an absentee-tax, and £90,000 by an income-tax,
and let the balance “slide ” for the present. Pro-
bably, however, the Council will reject the land
and absentee taxes, and, it is just possible, the
income-tax as well, and then the sow that was
washed will return to wallow in another issue of
Treasury bills, as usual.

A LOSING GAME.
FIRST UNEMPLOYED: “ Bad biz this beggiri',
Bill. You asks for bread , an’ they
gives yer a stone.”
Second Ditto: “ Naw fear; I asks fer
bread, hut I've lost two stone since I
started.”
Hume BlackV assurance that the Q.
Government will keep a register of farmers and
sugar-growers prepared to instruct pupil immi-
grants in their respective callings, is a well-studied
bid for the patronage of the “ home gentry,” who
know not what to do with their superabundant
boys.
Hardaore, M.L.A., has created a sensation
in Bananaland Parliament by declaring that there
is flourishing on the Drummond Range a
squatter unknown to officialdom, who pays no
rent, and whose squattage is mapped out in no
official records. Who?
Maoriland, after a seven years’ trial of
railway management by commissioners, proposes
to revert to the old plan of political control.
Which doesn’t prove that the commissioner
system is bad, but only that M.L. didn’t get the
right sort of commissioners.
If the display of gorgeous militarism at the
opening of Vic. Parliament does not induce the
Assembly to enact that no Vie. soldier shall be
allowed to wear scarlet, gold-braid, gilt-buttons,
feathers, or metal ornaments of any sort, an
object-lesson is simply wasted upon it.
Under the Factories Bill, now passe
through S.A. Assembly, State inspectors can
enter any factory at any time. Overcrowd-
ing and ill-ventilation are made penal, hours of
employment are limited to 48 per week, and em-
ployment of children is absolutely prohibited.
The Q. Bill raising Parliamentary salaries
from .£l5O to £250 has passed the Assembly by 31
to 28—though many of the minority only voted
against it because they knew it secure. The in-
crease will unquestionably help the Labor-party,
but the Boodlewraith tail could not longer deny
itself.
The oldest and youngest members of the
Vie. Parliament, Berry and Cook, represent W.
Bourke Boroughs. One has little to learn, haying
sat for 30 years ; the other a lot. Cook is Radical.
His father, a staunch Conservative and Free-
trader, stood for this seat years ago and lost his
deposit.
The picked escort which Q. magistrate
Parry-Okeden took to coerce the West, under the
Buckshot Act, proved more dangerous to friends
than to alleged enemies. It brought up the
measles from Brisbane and gave them to the
regular police, who have been more or less in bed
ever since.
The statement has been seasonably revived
that it was the attractiveness of the Melbourne
Cup to N.S.W. legislators, some years ago, that
led to the defeat of a then Government proposal
that N.S. Wales should join the Federal Council.
The legislative mutilators of the present week are
now being awaited.
The return to Queensland of Forrest,
M.L.0., leaves Agent-General Garrick once more
master of the situation in London. One of MTI-
- most furious threats was that he would
drive Garrick back into his Little Pe.dlington
existence; and Forrest was a means to that end.
But MTlwraithism wanes.
Theuuseating of Mr. G. H. Greene for Gren-
fell, N.S.W., on the mere ground that an official
forgot to initial and puncture the voting papers at
one booth, suggests openings for astute electioneers
to manoeuvre their friends into polling billets, and
get them to “forget” trivialities prescribed by the
Act, so as to take chances in case of a first defeat.
Czar Alexander 111. died last week of
dropsy, kidney-disease, and fright—mostly fright
—at the ever-present Nihilist spectre, his last
words, as reported by the pious cable, being, “ I
am quite prepared.” What a satire on conven-
tional religion when a Czar, with all the ghosts of
Siberia and Saghalien waiting to gibber at him on
the other side of the Styx, dies “ quite prepared ” !
The book of class-legislation as written in
the North :
I. Special legislation to give the planter cheap servile
labour.
IT, Special legislation to give him cheap State-subsidised
mills.
111. (In the near future) Special legislation to supply
manure to exhausted land and to eradicate cane disease.
IV. Pensions to all broken-down planters.
N.S.W. Govt., which last year paid Syd-
ney University .£17,000 odd as endowment, &c.,
paid also ,£l6OO fees for the students in training
for employment by the Education Department.
Considering all things, an economical Minister
for Public Instruction might have students taught
free, or know the reason why. A leech will drop
off when it is gorged, but the University seems
always greedy.
Federation, according to Chief Justice
Madden, is now much in want of someone to
argue on the other side of the question. This
great truth was announced in The Bulletin fully
a year ago. Federation makes no progress
because all parties have given in their nominal
adhesion to the principle; therefore, no party can
dish any other party by upholding it. Under the
party system—the system of Government by Con-
tradiction—the cause which has no opponent is
lost.
The Newcastle (N.S W.) coalminer is sot
down officially as earning 7s. 6d. per day. But, In
the first place, he is obliged to pay out of his 7s. 6d'
for powder, light and tools—say, ordinarily,fid.per
day ; and next, three days’ work a week Is the
estimated average of the miners during the last
two years. A miner, instead of making 455. a
week, as official figures imply, makes merely a
trifle over 20s.
There would, it is said, have been severe
retrench mint in a certain N.S.W. Govt. Depart-
TEE SWAGGIE'S TIMEPIECE.

round about here ? ”

ment had not the ex-Ministerial head, just before
leaving office, made an adroit move : he appointed
an immediate relation of an incoming Minister to
a billet in that Dept. The new Ministry could
hardly dismiss his earlier appointments without
sending off his last. Up to date there has been

Ex-Premier Patterson (Vic.) announces
himself in favour of “leasing the railways and
reducing freights.” The Vic. railways don t pay
just now, and if any Fat Man syndicate leases
them it will naturally proceed to make them pay.
It can only do this by raising freights, or reducing
wages, or greatly increasing the hours of labour,
or, most probably, by all three combined. There-
fore Patterson’s policy is a contradiction in
terms, and another proof of the muddle-headedness
of its’ author. Also, it is 17 more reasons why
the butcher of Oastlemaine should never again be
entrusted with the destinies of anything larger
than a meat-cask.
John Willard, M.P. for the Tweed, N.S.
Wales, arrested at Brisbane charged with stealing
receipts for registered letters, etc., had things
made just as uncomfortable for him as the Govt,
knew how. He was arrested on warrant, not
charged on summons; he was arrested in the even-
ing, too late to get bail; and next day the police
opposed the granting of bail altogether. Willard,
during his Brisbane career, was a noted opponent
of Q. Govt., and helped to_ start the Democratic
Chronicle —a paper which points out Govt,
deficiencies with annoying frequency. Now
Boodlewraith has got its opponent down, and is
proceeding to jump on him with both feet.
An Adelaide journalist wrote to Melb.
Argus protesting against the idea that Lord
Rosebery was bombastic and insulting to France
in his recent allusion to “ Agincourt.” Rosebery
spoke on St. Crispin’s Day, ‘’the anniversary of
the great battle,” and what more natural than his
reference to Hingland’s glory?—asks the Jingo
correspondent. But, then, every day is the anni-
versary of an event that happened long ago, and
the event, as a rule, was something which a Prime
Minister takes particular care not to mention.
Charles the First was beheaded on January 30,
the Battle of Hastings occurred on October 14,
the date of the Battle of Bannockburn was June
25, and so forth; yet Lord Rosebery, making a
speech on either of those days, wouldn’t trot out
the British lion
The retirement of N.S.W. Railway Com-
missioner Eddy at the end of his term of engage'
ment next year will be a public misfortune, for the
odds are a kangaroo to a paddymeloii that no one
half as good and masterful will be found to take
his place. His management of the lines has earned
him the esteem of every unbiassed man in tliecoiiw
raunity | and the best witnesses to his value are
the execrations with which Ills name is greeted by
baffled boodlers. If money can persuade Com-
missioner Eddy to remain, money should not be
spared.

A REQUIEM.
Toll the bell, the Czar is dead !
Nations mourn a god departed :
Eyes of “ Germany ” are red,
“France,” alas ! is broken-hearted ;
“ Austria ” is weeping loud,
“ Hingland’s” nose is hardly drier,
“Sweden’s ” heart by grief is bowed —
Writes the babbling cable-liar.
Toll the bell! ay, let it ring, >
Drowning foolish Tory screeches,
Telling how a Russian king
Wasn’t slaughtered in his breeches.
Lucky man ! the Czar is dead,
Long by Nihilists affrighted,
Crown another in his stead,
That he may get dynamited.
Toll the bell! the people scoff,
Though it move the ruling classes,
No mad monarch’s “ popping off ”
Agitates the weary masses.
Toll the bell, for want and woe
Reigning mid those toilers serried !
Kings can come, and kings can go
Till the last be dead and buried.
New Ideas.
We have had our ages of stone and bronze and i-o
on through increasingly improved grades of
mechanical genius, and now, when people ought
to have something approaching an intelligent
acquaintance with the phenomena of their own
bodies in health and disease, we are at the end of
the century of lotions. Some dyspeptic people
have disfiguring blotches and pimples on the face,
actually resultant from an hereditary blood taint
or a partial blood poison. What else do they
expect T At any rate they attempt to destroy the
skin disease by the use of lotions and cosmetics
and alleged “ beaQtifiers,” and as these block the
pores of the skin, the result is precisely the same
as in the case of the man who shut his flood-gates
to relieve a collapsing dam. Obviously, the only
sensible and reflective way of curing a disease
resulting from blood impurities is to correct the
cause, and this Warner’s Safe Cure does. Skin
disease is not really a disease, but merely a symp-
tom of one. While you are foolishly wasting
time with local applications, which would have
just as much effect if applied to a brick wall, a
disorganised stomach is calling for assistance, or
the poisoned kidneys are crying out for Warner’s
Safe Cure. The true method of treatment in
order to secure a beautiful, clear and transparent
skin, is to keep the natural channels of the system,
or sewers which carry off the vyaste products,
perfectly free from stagnation. The natural .chan-
nel for the escape of the waste and effete products is
through the kidneys, and the only guarantee of
freedom from eruptions upon the skin is to maintain
healthy action of these organs. Nature will
accomplish what artificial means always fail to do.
Nature has her own method and her own way of
doing things. Therefore do not attempt to im-
prove upon her methods. Keep the kidneys free
from the poisons which taint the blood and produce
blotches, boils, pimples, and blemishes. Use
Warner’s Safe Cure freeljrupoh the first indication
of any roughness of the skin, and we will guarantee
that the complexion will soon be restored to a
perfect condition.
INSURING CIGARS.
A rather good story appears in the Swiss Trade
Journal, devoted to the interests of tobacco. It
appears that an ingenious, but economical smoker,
with a weakness for good cigars, purchased a
thousand of Frossard’s Oavours, and forthwith
insured them. Then he set to work to smoke
them, and, having consumed the last, demanded
750 inks, from the insurance company, on..the
.ground that the whole of the insured stock ha 1
been destroyed by fire. The court decided in his
favor, whereupon the company brought an action
for conspiracy, accused him of having intention-
ally set fire to the cigars, and obtained his com-
mittal to prison for three months. The story may
be true, or it may not. At any rate, Frossard’s
Cavour Cigars are the best value ever produced in
Australia ; they are mild and aromatic, and sold
in packets of eight for one shilling.

Let us look out for another boom of arti-
ficial “ prosperity.” This is the revolution of the
cycle—Boom ; bust ; remorse ; evangelism ; fresh
crop of hypocrites; confidence-trickeries ; boom
again. Australia is just finishing with the evan-
gelistic sowers, the fresh crop of hypocrites is
warranted to spring up like mushrooms. There
are yet some forgotten confidence-tricks to play,
and the “ mostly fools ” are just about slumbering
off again.
# * #
“Society” tea-shops increase apace in
Sydney. There are now four of these useful and
elegant institutions, all presided over by really
nice people, and at one of them are three
daughters of a once wealthy and always-respected
politician now holding office.
mm*
The Melb. spiritualists are demanding a
test-seance from the leg-shrinking Mrs. Mellon.
The same society had “ successful ” test perform-
ances from a man who afterwards went to Maori-
land with high credentials. He and his sister
used to charge from £5 to £7 a night—and get it.
He mediumised and she “led the circle.”
Spiritualists who defraud the public in Maoriland
are arrested by the police, and this was their fate.
A plain-clothes bobby struck a matcli at a seance
and clutched the medium, in whose possession was
a long strip of muslin, a bottle of phosphorised
oil and other “properties.” And of such are all
the professional “mediums.”
# * #
Investigators of “ spiritualistic ” manifesta-
tions are safe so long as their mental position
approximates that of a drinky patient to whom the
present writer once brought the late Dr. Forteseue,
of Sydney. “Well,” said the doctor, “what’s
the matter?” “ Oh, doctor,” replied the patient,
“whenever I shut my eyes a man comes and
whispers into my ear : ‘ Cut your throat, cut your
throat! ’ ” “ And do you really believe that any-
body’s there?” asked the doctor. “Oh, no,”
replied the patient, “ I know perfectly well it’s a
delusion.” “ That's all right,” remarked Dr.
Forteseue, firing into the drinky gentleman 30grs.
chloral.
* # *
Apropos spiritual seances. There is
in Melb. a “ medium ” (formerly a hook-
fiend), whose Sunday night seances, at a “bob
a nob,” are opened by his greeting the audi-
ence with “ Goot eefening, fronts” (influence of a
North American Indian spook). He then prays
in common Australese. He converses with certain
spooks in broad Irish, others in Scotch, and finally
prays in a language specially understood, no
doubt, by the Supreme Being, but utterly
unknown to linguists. Then there is in Melb. a
woman medium, who trots out the Duke of Wel-
lington every Sunday night at 6d. a time, and
convinces the most sceptical that Wellington was
an illiterate boor, as he loudly commands the
British troops to “Hup, Guards, and Hat ’em ! ”
Still, the “ bobs ” and “ tanners ” roll in !
* * •
Last week The Bulletin printed a photo,
of Mrs. Mellon and her reincarnated child-spook,
“Cissie.” It will be noted that there is nothing
in the appearance of Cissie inconsistent with the
theory that the reincarnated spirit’s face is merely
a mask; which theory moreover is strengthened
by the fact that the white wrappings do not
appear to conceal a veritable human form, but
rather a clumsily-built up sort of scarecrow.

It is said that certain high judicial and
other dignitaries are forming a committee to
preside over the next Mellon test-stance. Thes
potentates intend to altogether exclude the press.
The test should soon eventuate, for several people
who have lately visited Mrs. Mellon affirm that
the lady looks as well and lively as ever,

Dear Bulletin, —Apropos your notice of
the many-titled potentates of Sydney College of
Music. A few years ago a shingle, inscribed
“Professor Blank, M.D., L.C.M.8.G.,” was
hung out in diseased Rockhampton, and the
scientist’s business was brisk until he was hauled
before the bench by the Medical Board to show
his diplomas. He had no diplomas. “Then
wlnt do your affixes mean?” asked the Bench.
It wis very simple—“ Late Curator of the Mary-
borough Botanical Gardens,” replied the indig-
nant accused. “ And, pray, what does the ‘ M.D.’
mean?” “Oh,” said the great healer, “that
means Money Down.”—L.L.
• # *
At the Mayor of Melbourne’s ball, last
week, the vice-regal set included a lady (“dressed
accordin’ ”) whose knighted husband could only
afford to pay a shilling in the £ a year or two ago.
When Mrs. Halfpenny has appeared at Fleming-
ton in a gold bonnet trimmed with clusters of
diamonds bigger than pigeon eggs, and Mrs.
Silver Mace keeps a separate kerridge and
pair for each day of the week, Melbourne will
begin to think that confidence is quite restored.
Cheek is only another name for confidence.
Dear Bulletin, —Bray, the Woolloomoo-
loo (N.S.W.) snake-man, was right. This is going
to be a glorious summer for snakes. Already,
thus early in the season, we fellows down here see
dozens daily.—Yours, Echo Home (for Inebriates),
Middle Harbor.
* * •
Hospital Saturday in Sydney —£1675;
Hospital Sunday, £264. The churches stood out
of the movement on the ground that its promoters
desecrated the Sawbath ; therefore, the saints
didn’t even take up hospital collections in their
temples. “We have the sick of our own congre-
gations to care for,” cries the Rev. Crosseye
Upturn; “ besides that, we have so manyoalls on
our charitable consideration, and the finger of
Providence has smitten the vineyards.” It was
reserved for ’94 to send parsons like Dropsytotal
Boyce complaining that hospital-canvassers defiled
the sanctity of the Sawbath, thus once more phari-
saically provoking the question ; “Is it lawful to
do good on the Sabbath-day, or to do evil ?”
* * *
In fining the publisher of Melb. Age £l5O
for contempt of Court in printing David Syme’s
famous speech, while not fining David anything
for making the speech, Judge Williams practically
decides that a statement may be quite harmless
when made verbally before a large crowd, yet
when the same statement is printed in a news-
paper it becomes altogether unlawful. In a word,
that a newspaper has less privilege than is enjoyed
by the live-meat citizen. This is still another
definition of newspaper law.

Age, 20th Oct. :
"VT EE OLE WOMAN wanted, quick, able to make
■IN children’s clothes; Is. 6d. day. Wonga, Age
office.
Eighteenpence a day! It is easy to picture
“ Wonga.” A long, gaunt female with a flat chest
and a penchant for tea-meetings—one exceedingly
rough on “vice” and the tendency of young girls
to give up their honest employment to lead a gay

N.S. Wales has
an “ equity ” - case,
Hubert v. Brown,
which has been in
the local courts ever
since 1855.
* *
Once more N.S.
Wales Banco Court
gets home on the
rights of juries. In the
slander case, Buckle
v. Hordern, the jury
awarded plaintiff
£4OO damages. De-
fendant applied for a
new trial. “ Will
you,’’ said Banco to
plaintiff, “ take £3OO
and we’ll not allow
Hordern to harass
you with a new trial?”
Buckle demurred.
“ Well,” continued
Banco, “ we’ll grant
a new trial, but if,
within a certain
period, you agree to
knock off £lOO we’ll
quash our order.”
And so the case
stands. But if it be
lawful for Banco to
do this, what prevents
it knocking off half,
f r three-quarters, or
the whole of the
amount assessed by a
jury ? And what is
the use of a new
trial, if the case be
prejudged in favour
of one of the parties?
Some friends of the wife of a retired A«s-
tralian brewer recently made an afternoon call on
rat (say) Porche iter Gate. The tea-table was
adorned with a splendid gold service. When the
visitors came in of course they were asked if they
had had tea, and when one of them expressed a
desire for a cup, the lady of the house rang the
bell and said to the footman : “Joseph, bring in
the silver-service.” True Arifiifo Australian form.

« * *
On the morning after the terrible Redfern
railway catastrophe the S.M. Herald thus headed
its first leading article :
The shocking accident which yesterday occurred at Red-
fern railway-station has, not unreasonably, produced a
profound sensation in the city.
Could any more inane sentence be penned to ex-
press, in what poses as the leading daily paper of
the city, the sentiment of the community ? “ Not
unreasonably !” That phrase is a sort of epitome
of the flabby morale of a journal which
But we forbear. A day may come! Perhaps
when some future heir to the headship of the
house of Fairfax runs off with and marries the
stalwart and dashing cook of the “Mister James”
of the period, the resultant generation may
give the leading articles an inspiriting vim instead
of the soul-destroying phrases of white-choker-
dom.
* * *
The Redfern railway disaster and the
Wairarapa wreck were two great opportunities
for the Sydney evening papers. Both these events
came along at an hour which left the morning
papers badly in the lurch, and the journals which
cast a gloom over the lunch-hour made the very
best they knew of the opportunity. In the case of
the Wairarapa the information was exceedingly
scanty, but the Australian Star padded it out
by giving the names of the passengers in big
capitals with only one name in each line, then
a list of the drowned members of the crew,
also in big capitals with one name in each
line, and after that a complete list of the
crow—these items made a column and a-half
altogether. Also, the manifest of the ship was
published right down to sixteen rolls of matting
and some bones. Then the News went one better
by publishing the list of passengers twice over
and making three columns of them altogether,
and issuing a map of the Hauraki Gulf, and the
same old manifest right down to the matting and
the bones. Meanwhile, the morning papers were
getting ahead on the railway-accident, for whereas
the Star and News only killed one passenger and
scalded a dozen or two, the Herald and Tele-
GRAVH came out in the small hours with seven
killed, a plan of the railway line, and an article
on the appearance of the injured, whom the re-
porters had been allowed to more or less, harry in
one of the hospitals. Then the evening papers
came along again a few hours later and killed
another man, and had an inquest, and next day
the morning papers killed three more. Altogether,
it was a great time.

The dreadful Redfern railway-accident has
started the fish and gill-bird liars on another tack.
To hold everybody who claims to have been a
passenger the unfortunate train must have reached
from Sydney to Parramatta; and at least 17,000
persons besides were going to travel by it and
turned back to get their pipes or kiss the baby
Human nature is a fearful and wonderful thing.

One intending passenger per Wairarapa,
from Sydney, got so drunk near the hour of
departure that he missed his passage. Two poor
Salvation army girls, who were passengers, were
drowned.

Dear Bulletin,-
The Pastoralists’
Union, formed for the
purpose of obstructing
unionist shearers, is
just as unmoral a thing
as the Shearers’ Union,
formed to obstruct
unionist pastoralists;
but the pastoralists
don’t use filthy lan-
guage to the wives and
daughters of river-
steamer owners, nor do
they, as a rule, behave
as what the world calls
“ blackguards.” Just
after the burning of
the Rodney 50 unionist
shearers passed a hut
nearTolarno. A woman
was being confined in
the hut, and the mid-
wife asked these brutes
to have respect for
her condition. But,
although the woman
was in agonies, they
howled horrible, filthi-
nesses at her door, and
forced the midwife to
stand with a light to
show them the road
when at last they de-
cided to leave. The
majority must come on
top, but if it is such
a foul-mouthed insani-
tary majority as the
present labor mob is,
the minority, which is
simply fatheaded and
not disreputable, will
prove the better thing.
The educated democrat
is bran-new from the'
foundry of God, but the
ignorant democrat !
He leaves a dark-brown
taste in the month.—
Randolph the Reck-

Poor Bishop Webber, who went ’Ome
vowing to raise .£50,000 or never, never to return,
is coming back with less than £lO,OOO. Among
his contributors he counts De Satge, Rawson, and
Henry Gibbon, the last subscribing £lO5. By
the way, the residuary legatees of the late James
Gibbon took something like half-a-million out of
Queensland. This is how Bishop Webber put it;
“ I say profits go to loan and mortgage com-
panies, and not to people who produce wealth.
A good season in Queensland means more balls in
the West End and suburban gaieties.” Verily, a
prophet has arisen in Israel 1
* # #
The Melb. evening Amalgam tells a touch-
ing story in connection with the wreck of the
Wairarapa. A young lady-passenger hailing from
Melbourne, before leaving the city of smells, con-
sulted two fortune-tellers who both said she was
going upon a voyage from which she would never
return. “On learning this,” says the paper, with
mulish unconsciousness, “Miss M'Kellar tried to
induce one of her sisters to make the visit.” How-
ever, Miss M’Kellar ventured and was amongst
the saved. This story is supposed to attest the
marvellous powers of the local witches, but the
point is not obvious, and The Bulletin gives the
anecdote in the interests of religion, because of
the moral it points to sisters generally.
# * *
The lesson cannot be too well learnt that
when a ship strikes on rocks and the masts
do not go at the first shook, they are likely
to be the last things left standing—if the ship
is firmly fixed. So the Wairarapa passengers
found, while those who trusted to the bridge—
an exposed structure comparatively slightly sup-
ported and offering a large surface to the leverage
of the waves—were swept away along with it.

When the R.C. Church means business it
would scorn to adopt the canting subterfuges of
its Protestant rival. Outside St. Patrick’s Cathe-
dral, Melbourne, stands a huge advt. board,
announcing in white letters, on a bright blue
ground, an Art Union for the benefit of the build-
ing fund—Prizes, £2OOO. At half-a-mile distance
the eye of speculative Christianity can feast upon
those tempting figures—£2ooo. As soon as Union
No. 1 has filled the clerical Tatt. will open
another sweep, probably.
Further, the Archbishop desires that the goods
at the fair shall represent something approxi-
mating solid value for cash, and that the sales-
women will give the public a chance to escape
buying the things they don’t want. Most im-
portant point of all, the Archbishop—mindful,
perhaps, of those poor johnnies who took tea in
Nellie Stewart’s tent—exhorts the young ladies to
“return the full amount of change.”

genially admitted everything,

The recent Vie. seizure
of clerical vestments for import
duty was paralleled a few years
ago in one of the South Sea
islands. Deputy-Commissioner
Romilly bailed up a French
priest’s impedimenta at Rotumah and demanded
duty. “ Mais, monsieur,” cried the priest as each
article was tallied, “c’est pour Dieu.” “I asked
mm at last,” narrates Romilly, “if a hhd. of
was ‘ pour Dieu’ and received the reply,
Mais, oui, c’est tout pour Dieu.’ ” But Romilly
exacted his duty.

As usual, Yic. gubernatorial party, includ-
ing a spindle-shanked and hairv-kneed Cale-
donian, rolled up at Melb. Mayor’s ball about an
hour late, and departed 40miu. after. What
depths of “loyalty” Victorians must wallow in
when they slobber over the “dear Countess” who,

torians,” wrote Francis Adams, “they’re such
snobs—they’re worse than English people.”
* • *
Rev. John Fordyce’s self-denying ordin-
ance, promulgated before the Congregational
Union, not to start a church in any locality in
Australia where any Christian church is already
at work, may not be sound doctrinally, but is,
from a business point of view, most sensible. A
moderate living for one parson means straits for
two and starvation for three, with the outcome of
extermination for all. The country newspaper
press should study Pastor Fordyce’s ordinance.
Business men in general should give it a thought.
But, doctrinally, does it not mean—first make
sure of your living ; then propagate your dis-
tinctive creed ?

* # «
Poor old Fitz-Gibbon, of Melbourne, has
met with a new disaster. Another Fitz-Gibbon
has arisen, in Calcutta, to state that his (the Cal-
Photo, by Falk Studios] [Sydney.
Miss MAY POLLARD .
cutta man’s) elder brother is the real “White
Knight of Kerry,” and that the Melbourne
Gibbon has no claim to the title whatsoever. So
far as can be gathered from much infuriated Irish
correspondence on the subject, the original white
knight, or “ Mac-an-L’san Riddery,” came from
the Fitz-Gibbon branch of the Desmond
Geraldines, and was killed by the Mao Oarthy
More at Kenmare, and buried at Ardfinnan,
leaving a widow named Norah Ny Connor.
Thereafter the story vanishes into a mist
of genealogy, through which can be faintly
descried here and there the huge hairy
legs and battleaxes of old caterans named
Malaohi Hoolan or Cornelius M'Dermott,
who belabored each other furiously among the
bogs in days of old. These individuals seem to
have pounded each other out of shape through
four centuries, and at the end of that period they
all died, and the direct line of the White Knights
became extinct, and thereafter Fitz-Gibbon’s ances-
tors and the ancestors of the rival Gibbon both
claimed the barren distinction, and they seem to
have died and been buried in Galway amid the
general grief of all the adjacent Hooligans and
O’Donnells without ever coming to a definite
settlement. Irish genealogy is an interesting
study when you don’t get too much of it at once.
The Bulletin trusts that the Melb. Fitz-Gibbon
will prevail in the present controversy. It would
be refreshing to know for certain that one Aus-
tralian knight was really “white.”

The Y.M.C.A. Hall, Bourke-st., Melb.
(original cost, .£55,000), has passed into the
possession of the Harmy for the sum of £19,000,
and the good young men are out in the cold. No
institution in Melbourne is so typical of the spirit
of the boom as the Y.M.C.A., but its members
and leaders mistook the boom for the Grace of
God.

G. M‘K.; Mr. Henry’s action in grabbing the so-called
ghost at the Sydney spiritualistic seance and demon-
strating the fraud which was being worked was decidedlv
vigorous and unconventional, but it was no more
“ brutal” or wicked than is the action of the surgeon
who extirpates a cancer. Nor was Mr. H. guilty of any
“ breach of private hospitality,” for the affair was public
in that reporters had been invited to attend (and aid
in bamboozling the public as on previous occasions). In
helping to smash the idea that people can conjure up and
commune with the ghosts of their dead, Mr. Henry has
performed an inestimable public service «nd kept out of
the lunatic asylums many people who were trembling on
the borderland of insanity Mesach: The Bulletin had
no “pecuniary interest” in “ relentlessly opposing " the
Colo Valley grab. The Bulletin’s opposition to that big
reach-out was simply inspired by the fact that the wire-
pullers most prominently engaged therein were nearly all
wild-eyed Single-taxers who, after ranting for years and
years about the absolute necessity of conserving for the
people the “natural opportunities” of the land, and so
on, took the very first chance to convulsively clutch for
themselves and gang the “ natural opportunities ” of the
Colo Valley. The sight of that local-preacher-looking
aggregation of lobbyists night after night in the Parlia-
mentary vestibules and passages was too much for Tub
Bulletin’s liver. Look here: If a Melbourne band of
company-promoters can hope to make a few hundreds of
thousands by turning over the Colo water-rights in the
London market, why shouldn’t the Government market
those rights and dump the proceeds into the Treasury ?
The secret of the missionary business :
Sending out new missionaries is the life of the work.
Each missionary by his constituency brings in more than
he takes out.

IN THE SUPREME COURT OF QUEENS-
LAND.
Matrimonial Causes Jurisdiction.
The CHIEF JUSTICE
Between
CHARLOTTE CROSS
Petitioner
And
WALTER HENRY CROSS
Respondent.
To the abovenamed WALTER HENRY CROSS
formerly of Brisbane Senior Sub-Inspector of
Traffic.
TAKE NOTICE that on the twenty-sixth day
of September one thousand eight hundred and
ninety-four the abovenamed CHARLOTTE
CROSS filed a petition in this Court against you
praying that the marriage solemnised between her-
self and you at Southampton in Hampshire in
England on the thirteenth day of January one
thousand eight hundred and eighty-five might; be
dissolved on the grounds of desertion cruelty and
adultery on your part; and further praying that
she might have the custody of the two infant chil-
dren of the said marriage. AND FURTHER
TAKE NOTICE that on the twenty-sixth day pf
September aforesaid a citation was issued out of
the Registry of this Court on the part of the peti-
tioner commanding you within one calendar month
after the service thereof on you inclusive of the
day of such service to cause an appearance to be
entered for you in the said Court in the matter of
such petition and that in default of such appear-
ance the hearing and determination of the charges
in the said petition would proceed in your absence.
AND FURTHER TAKE NOTICE that by an
order made by the said Court on the twenty-second
day of October one thousand eight hundred and
ninety-four it was ordered that substituted service
of the said petition and citation on you be effected
by posting copies of the same up in the Registry
and the Entrance Hall of the Supreme Court at
Brisbane and by advertising this notice in the
Queensland Government Gazette and in the
Kiama Independent the Sydney Morning
Herald the Sydney Bulletin the Melbourne Age
and the West Australian Newspapers and that
compliance with the said order should be deemed
good and sufficient service of the said petition and
citation on you and that service should be deemed
to have been effected as of the date of the publica-
tion of the last of the said advertisements.
Dated this twenty-sixth day of October 1894.
UNMACK & FOX
Queen-street Brisbane
Solicitors for the abovenamed Petitioner.

Sydney, 6th November, 1894.
Derby Day at Fleminyton.
The V.R.O. Spring meeting, writes “ Sappho
Smith,” opened auspiciously at Flemington, last
Saturday, with the Derby, when the big race-
course was just comfortably crowded. Though
the back-blockers are always in Melbourne some-
time beforehand, they rarely break the sporting
ice until Cup day, so that the Derby is always a
pleasant, sit-down sort of festival, to which one
can confidently wear one’s pinchiest shoes in the
blissful certainty of being able to find a seat.
Flemington looked very lovely on Saturday. The
lawn grass was long and fresh, and the Flem-
ington flowers had made a special effort for the
occasion.
There was quite an overpowering show of vice-
regality on the box-seat, so to speak. Lady
Hopetoun looked altogether fetching in a pinky
silk, completely covered in cream chiffon, with
aecordeon-pleated sleeves and small rosettes of
pink. A floral toque of forget-me-nots, with a
wide-awake pink rose and a cream silk parasol,
completed a neat turn-out.
Lady Duff wore salmon pink and a black toque,
and looked even more weary than usual. Lady
Norman and Lady Glasgow sported raopoke hues,
out of compliment to the defunct Czar, ’twas said.
As both these ladies had to travel some distance
to the Cup, it seemed rather ghoulish of them to
pack up a weeping frock apiece, in cold blood,
so as to be prepared for the monarch’s death
during Cup week. Apropos of the Czar, I have
just heard tell of of a Victorienne whose husband
had once suffered and recovered from the Imperial
complaint (lucky man !) and who wrote, offering
her services to the Royal invalid. “I have nursed
my husband through it, and am more qualified to
nurse His Majesty than anybody,” wrote this
modest Melbourne matron, who doubtless expected
to return crowned with laurel as the Duchess of
Komskikoff.
Quite a scurry of vice-regal maidens helped to
cram the Governor’s box. Miss Grace Norman,
wore a strawberries-and-crearn frock ; Miss Kate
and Miss Heather Duff, fawn and pink and
bright yellow respectively ; Miss M. Bowden
Smith had a floral gown with pink braces. But
I offend official etiquette in mentioning these
damsels before admiringly chronicling the success
of Lord Hopetoun’s new spring toilet of the
palest shade of tabby-cat grey, gloves and hat en
suite, and tricky boutonniere of saffron rosebuds.
Gov. Kintore, who looks less and less like a
blue-ribbon disciple every year, sported iron-grey
with such a faded buttonholer that it looked like
a gage d'amour. Lord Glasgow, Viscount Gor-
manston, and Gov. Duff were the rest of the
social rulers on view.
Sydneyites wore rather weak in point of num-
bers on Derby Day. Mrs. James Aberoorn White
wore a black moire gown with three albescent
plumes in her bonnet. Mrs. Tom Slattery sported
blue and white checked silk with fal-de-rals of
black chiffon and jet. Mrs. Walter Hall wore
blue and white checked silk with cornflowers in
her bonnet ; Miss Burdekin was airy in floral
muslin fixed with green and pink silk; Miss
Marcia Cox wore hailstone muslin and a leghorn
hat trimmed with crimson roses, and Miss Felicia
Manning wore a neat grey suit.
I discovered a new male boutonniere—an adorn-
ment a span across of small strawberries and
leaves. It suggested dessert. Cyril Tyler turned
up at the Derby ; the man who photo’d him in a
surplice with his eyes turned heavenwards and a
big hymn-book ought to have seen the little
cherub in shooting knickerbockers, with an eye
like a hawk, getting the latest scratchings in his
race-book.
Taking Derby dressing, lock, stock and barrel,
I can’t say I made many sartorial discoveries.
The very newest things were the Infinitesmal
u nbrellas—long, thin gamps with a tricky bow
on the handle, folding up as small as a cane.
Nellie Stewart, attended by Mr. Musgrove (with
a beauty-spot on his neck) and a chaperone, wore
a new combination of biliously green checked silk,
with a cavalier collar of butter-color lace and
aggravations of black chiffon and jet. I liked
Mrs. George Chirnside’s trappings as well as any
—cream figured silk gauze over white silk, and a
black Hamlet hat. Mrs. Rupert Clatke, in butter-
fly-colors, was a startling contrast to the rest of
the family, who wore mourning, of course.
Writes “Van M.”
The woman who did not look her best on Derby -
day had only herself or her modiste to thank.
Mother Nature, in benignant mood, bestowed
bright sunshine, blue skies, soft, unparched grass—
a perfect setting for a costume pageant.
However, even literary tourists, those critical
Snarley Yows, allow that the “colonial female”
has a successful, if somewhat gay, knack of dress-
ing, and this year’s racegoers have, so far, not
belied their reputation. Saturday’s gowns were
cool and pretty, the light summer silks being, in
my opinion, the most effective. A silk has a
dainty dignity all its own, not attainable by the
most ambitious orepon or cotton, and yet, in soft,
spring tints, looks innocent and virginal enough
for the robe of a newly-materialised angel.
The Govt. House contingent arriving at Flem-
ington, with the usual trimmings of postillions
and outriders, looked enviably festive dismount-
ing and trooping into the vice-regal box, which,
in a society novel, would thereafter have re-
sounded with the mirth and silvery laughter of
the highborn company. Youth at the prow and
pleasure at the helm, and all that sort of thing,
don’cher know. But fact, generally speaking, is
duller than fiction, and on this occasion the cre-
tonne draperies of the holy of holies, most of the
time, rustled only to the mild chit-chat of a hen-
convention, the men-folk merely dropping in
officially, so to speak. Certainly, the ladies were
led forth in turn for walking exercise, the matrons
by their Excellencies, the maidens by “the
suites.” Still, this dissipation notwithstanding,
the feminine share of the vice-regal outing bore
any aspect but that of mad revelry.
There were exceptions, of course, to the general
chaos of dullness. Melbourne’s vice-regal dame
was in her usual good racing form, which doesn’t
imply that she hugged the society of her own sex
any more closely than usual; while a pretty girl
in salmon-pink, said to be a relative of Lord Kin-
tore, also had a gay time, though quite indepen-
dently of things equine.
Lady Hopetoun’s acoordeon- plaited cream
chiffon, worn over shrimp-pink silk, had its upper
skirt draped and looped in the bunchy fashion of
yore, while an elfin garden of forget-me-nots,
with a pink moss rosebud waving its head above
the blue, constituted a sweet little bonnet,
The Governor himself was a harmony in grey
an( j lived well up to his dove-like exterior, not
ru ffli ng his plumage at the constantly reiterated
ca t e chism regarding the welfare of his family-tree,
Qn the contrary, he testified in the joyous manner
one une arthing a new topic, firstly, that to the
best of his belief the warm brown of the Countess’
complexion is no longer mottled by scars;
seC ondly, that Lord Hope, if you please, is quite
W ell thank you and has become a polo enthusiast;
and, lastly, that little Benjamin, the only Austra-
ij an edition of the House of Hopetoun (him of the
ru ddy locks), still thrives (and no doubt howls
an d has colds in his head) after the manner of
infants.

Their ladyships Duff and Gormanston s
up well in pink and petunia, respectively, and, as
usual, everyone was crying out about Gov.
Robinsons ‘splendid face. But people always
do discover you re a professional beauty when
you’ve composed a song or made any kind ot a
splash, just as if one sort of success weren t
sufficient. Uninfluenced by the popular ver i ,
I hasten to lay meed of admiration at the shrine
of the Sydney pro-consul, on the ground that at
all events, he can’t be mistaken for an unusually
well-grown and prosperous actor,
A potentate who broke the monotony of the
vice-regal tableaux was a stout patriarch garbed
according to the methods of a superannuated
Dissenting Shepherd. His baggy black coat, gone-
over pants, and hat pressed well down on his
good baldhead, were so genially regardless of
appearances that he soon became as popular with
outsiders as he appeared to be with the ladies of
his own party.
Among the throng were Mrs. Rupert Clarke, in
blue silk and chiffon, her hat ablaze with carna-
tions; Miss Manning, daughter of the Sydney
Mayor, having a very quiet time in a grey tailor-
made costume; Mrs. Walter Coldhain, sweet and
tresh, in silver-grey silk. Pretty wives, like
other blessings, run in families. The clan which
has been fortunate enough to annex the last-raen-
tioned lady can boast of equally attractive
matrons, in all its scattered branches,
Mrs. George Chirnside’s white silk and grena-
dine was almost too colorless for its pale wearer ;
w },ile the bright yellow gown, worn by the hand-
some or jgi na i 0 f a portrait in the “Victorian
Artists,” went to the other extreme, almost
dazzling one with its brilliancy. Miss Madden
and her sister were not,at their best in limp floral
muslins, which, though becoming enough to the
f a j rj girlish faces of the wearers, were sadly lack-
j n g j n s t y l e an d smartness of cut.

j tg Melbourne Cup lip didn’t come off, save by remarking

mentioned won’t win, Tub Bulletin s tip, by not coming
f rom (.Jjg betting latterly, and might probably be des-

to 1) was second; Nada (l6 to 1), third. The only gleam

which was such a surprising case of accuracy that the

In 34 Melbourne Cup races only seven favorites
have got home. Among the winners one started at 100
to 1 ; three at 50 to 1; one at 40 to 1 ; two at 33 to 1; three
at 20 to 1; one ;at 18 tol; two at 16 to one; one at 14 to
I; one at 12 to 1: and four at 10 to 1. Which makes
n ; ne t een rank-outsiders among thirty-four winners.
Therefore, The Bulletin rises up after the event like the
voice of one in the wilderness who looks the stable door
upon that mill which will never grind with the water that
is past, and gives the following infallible recipe for spot-
ting a Cup winner: Write each horses name on a piece
of paper: put all the papers on the verandah-sill in the
gutl) an d back the animal that the first blow-fly alights

The Australasian, with all its opportunities
for obtaining “information,” named 18 horses for the six
races on Saturday, without putting a name to one winner.
* * * *
Carnage—he looks very handsome, and is, more-
over, in good, if not hard, condition —has developed a
vice which necessitates the performance of a mysterious
rite by the trainer before he can run for a race. This
secret was kept close, like all St. Albans secrets, until
Saturday, but it became common property after Munro
was seen handling Carnage in a particular way. The
most surprised persons on the ground were three ladies,
who came along to see the finishing touches put to “dear
Carnage.” They soon beat a discreet retreat.
» * » ♦
Although the V.R.O. stewards took four hours
to consider the Derby protest, there was no public objec-
tion to The Harvester’s victory. Among those who saw
the mSl6e, the majority considered that Malachite had
chiefly benefited by it. Dreamland’s jockey was forced
to stop riding for two or three seconds, and Bonnie Scot-
land received a buffet. But seeing that the other three
horses were “ collaring ” Bonnie Scotland at the time, Mr,
Gollan’s complaint went for very little.

Byron Moore, secretary Y.R.C., much to the
disgust of a crowd of other hangers-on, has expunged the
names of 192 bogus pressmen who in former years got
free press-tickets for the four days’ racing. It’s scarcely
creditable, but one mean Canadian had for years got
press-box tickets for Vic. race meetings as representative
of Arizona Kicker ! Another travelled as the Colorado
Clarion —both alleged papers being mere myths, invented
by certain ’Murkan humorists. This meeting, anyhow,
genuine pressmen are allowed to see something from the
press-box at Flemington.

The starting-machine will take 15 per cent, off
starter Watson’s lease of purgatory. It will enable him
to presently swear off swearing. On Saturday, for pro-
bably the first time in the world, the manner of a start
was rapturously applauded. The Derby horses left the
line like automata, haunch to haunch and neck to neck.
The lawn and hill and flat clapped honest admiration, a
thing seldom known on a racecourse.

* • * *
W. Brockwell, Stoddart’s star bat, represents
an English sporting paper on the Australian tour.
♦ * * ♦
Major Ben. Wardill, of Melb., went to Adelaide
to meet Stoddart’s team and was the hero of a very
“happy” speech made at the reception. Said Ben.,
“ We asked Stoddart to bring eight amateurs and five pro-
fessionals and we would have done them, by gad ; but he
has brought five amateurs and eight professionals and
they’ll do us, by gad I”
* * * *
Stoddart’s team look better in the flesh than
they do on paper. The remarkable thing about them is
their average height. Leaving out Briggs and Peel, they
are all six-footers. Humphreys is stout, grey haired, and
middle-aged, and will hardly be equal to continuous work.
Ford, the hitter, is about as tall as Bonnor and as thin as
Bruce. Brockwell, Albert Ward, and Richardson are all
big, powerful fellows and just the sort of men for a heavy
trip. Brockwell will be the success of the tour on his
form at practice ; but Ford, who is a left-hander and hits
hard, will be as popular with the public as “Billy” Bruce,
simply because the public love batsmen far removed from
the genus stonewaller. J. T. Brown, the Yorkshireman,
Albert Ward (Lancashire) and Brockwell (Surrey) are all
splendid safe bats, and will, with Stoddart, do most work.
All the team have a high opinion of Richardson’s bowling
and expect great things from the Surrey demon.
* » • •
The sensation of this English Eleven’s visit
may be the lob-bowling of Humphreys. We have never
had a real lob-bowler here, and Humphreys (now about
46 years old) has more than once played the deuce with
the peers of Australian batsmen.
* » * *
Stoddart’s amateurs and pros, keep up the old
obnoxious English tradition by staying at different hotels.
♦ ♦ ♦ ♦
Brown, of Stoddart’s team, is said to be the
finest “point” in the world—better even than Shrewsbury
or Walter Read.
* • * •
The present English team has more young
bloods than previous teams from “ ’ome,” and headed by
Stoddart they are the most dashing batsmen that have
visited Australia. Peel and Humphreys are their only
slow scorers, and for the Britishers to be all day at the
wickets means over 300 runs. In their first match against
the Gawler (S.A.) 18 they put up 368 runs—a great day’s
score against any 18.
♦ • * •
Editor Bulletin, —You say bookmakers have
to live. Yes, but not by giving odd* that are grossly un-
fair. I backed Devon for the Caulfield Cup and only got
20 to 1 about him from one of the best-known Melb. book-
makers. The day the ticket was dated the horse was
quoted at 100 to 1£ i n the Aas, and he was never less than
33 to 1. I was glad to see your remarks re Paris running
in Caulfield Stakes. A small owner would have had to
explain to the stewards.—Caradoc, Mildura.

Touts talk amusingly about the effect of foot-
plates upon a horse’s training gallop. So-and-so made
splendid time, “considering that he had his shoes on,”
says the early bird, as though he were allowing, at least,
three seconds per mile for the extra weight and encum-
brance. It is probable that many horses which run bare-
footed in Australia would travel faster on racing-plates,
