Writes “Victor":—The Harvester, much to
my surprise, has repeated The Admiral trick for Mr. Sam.
Cook ; had, however, Cobbity not been cast in his box the
Derby, I firmly believe, would have, through his instru-
mentality, been credited to Mr. Lloyd.
Weeks ago I informed Bulletin readers that
the chestnut son of Aberoorn could “ run rings ” round
Malachite, who, but for the bumping about he got some
250yds. from home, should have won. What a moral,
then, the Derby would have been for Cobbity 1 However,
might, could and would don’t count.
It is just on the cards that Dreamland has been
ruined for racing purposes by his hurried preparation.
He is but a big baby, and till five years of age “ the
shelf ” should be his position.
Moorite, who will yet prove himself the best of
his age, was judiciously scratched for the Derby. He
wants time, and, though he improves every day, it was
found impossible to send him to the post in Derby condi-
tion.
Gale ran lbs. below his true form in the Mari-
byrnong Plate, for, after looking all over a winner, he
suddenly collapsed. This son of Nautilus will yet turn
out a “hummer'” or I’m much mistaken. Challenger
will also see a better day.
Euroka, judging by his running into third place
behind Ruenalf and Escapade at Randwick, and The
Harvester and Malachite at Caulfield, is a likely winner of
something ere the week’s out.
Mr. Albert Stiarp, the discoverer of the
new Cannalin remedies for piles, announces that,
in future, there will be a single charge of one
pound for the six weeks course of treatment.
This will avoid delay in serious cases from remote
parts of the colonies, as a pound-note can be sent
where postal-order is not available. Applications
must be accompanied by a full description of the
case, and be addressed plainly to 257 George-st.,
Sydney, N.S.W.
AUSTRALIAN MUTUAL PROVIDENT
SOCIETY.
That another year is now drawing to a close is
brought forcibly before us by the advertisement of
the Australian Mutual Provident Society, which
appears in another column, reminding those who
are contemplating life assurance that the next
division of profits will take place as at 31st.
December next, and that all policies issued before
that date will participate therein. According to
the liberal conditions of that popular Society, a
policy now issued, and upon which a yearly pre-
mium has been paid, will be entitled to a full
year’s bonus at the end of the year. If the pre-
mium be paid half-yearly, then a half-year’s bonus
will be added, and so on. All bonuses vest imme-
diately a policy has been in force two years. The
amount of cash bonus divided for the year 1893
was nearly half-a-million sterling.
According to Turner, Ferris was never employed
as a professional bowler in England. He went into a part-
nership on Bristol Stock Exchange, and instead of being
fat and rosy, as is currently reported, is thinner than
ever. He has lost all his old form, too, with the ball.

The latest race-dream, Webb, editor of S.A.
Chkokiclb, lately dreamt that he applied to the Delphio
Oracle for the “ double '* for Caulfield and Melb. Cups.
He was told toget the nomination lists and, laying his ear
to each paper in succession, pass it along from the bottom
to the top under promise that the winner in each case
would pronounce its own name. This he did, and from
each list came distinctly the desired word. But, when he
woke up, though all the rest of the dream was clear and
he remembered that “ the double” had been told him, he
could not call the names to mind. The greatest trouble
of all is that he firmly believes the Oracle spoke truly.

In the West,
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
In the Land of Broken Promise, where the peace-
ful Darling flows,
And the rough and roving shearer, like a pilgrim,
yearly goes,
Where the roaming cattle wander, and the woolly
legions stray,
Now the gay and dashing trooper goes a-prancing
all the day.
For upon the lonely Darling, where the squatter
o’er his bales
Measures out the shearer’s portion with the old,
unequal scales,
Now the rough-and-ready shearer, with a dreadful
swear has sworn
In a warm and vivid diction, that he’ll never more
be shorn.
And his snipping shears are silent, with signi-
ficance profound,
While the fierce and sullen shearer’s prowling
moodily around,
And, like crows that scent the powder, all the
carrion blacklegs fly
From the deadly danger lurking in the corner of
his eye.
For his soul has waxed impatient as spring leaves
the wattle-gum,
And he yearns to send the blackleg to the realms
of Kingdom Come;
From the Darling flashing daily come grim mes-
sages of war,
Of the blazing hut and woolshed, and the shearer
steeped in gore.
Stalwart shearer, free and careless ! on thy hot,
impatient head
Fall like thunderbolts rebounding all thy whistling
balls of lead.
And the flame thou madly kind lest, by the breath
of bias fanned,
Forges but another weapon for the stronger hostile
hand.
Thou shalt profit by the lesson, yet thy learning
comes full late
That thy best and surest weapon is the strength
to watch and wait.
And beware thou, too, O shearer of the Darling,
blackleg-curst,
Lest thou answer at the calling of the roll in
Darlinghurst.
And before another season, go thou, shearer, and
repent
Of thy little faults and failings, and the cheques
so rashly spent;
Little dreamed you, little recked you, of the potency
of “ grub,”
When the rum was freely flowing in the Lamb-
down wayside “pub.”
Then, with'all thy forces marshalled, larder full
and idle shears,
Thou shalt face the foe in triumph and mayst
take him by the ears,
Till he lieth down before thee like the meek and
lowly lamb,
Though his inward salutation will be probably a
“ d n !”
And the tardy gates of justice then shall open
swing once more,
And you’ll stand again triumphant, full within
the temple door;
For the bolts, though long unyielding, yet they
open at a price,
When the hungry gods are greedy for the annual
sacrifice.
W.B.M.
According to the cable, London Times
urges that Britain should negotiate a new com-
mercial treaty with Japan, giving the Japanese, in
return for the free admission of British products,
entry to the colonies on the same basis as
Europeans. In other words, the Times proposes
that Britain should get certain valuable trading
privileges from Japan, and that the Japs should
have, as compensation, the right to ruin the labor
market of Australia. It was probably the bril-
liant Miss Flora Shaw who conceived this great
idea in the way of one-sided reciprocity. It seems
hardly possible that any other “colonial autho-
rity ” could be quite so genially fatuous and
obtuse.
Paddy M‘Caughan, of the Melb. “ Rialto,”
departed for Coolgardie the other day in a mar-
vellous costume, of the kind usually built for
amateur Alpine tourists. Togs are Paddy’s
supreme weakness at all times, but his Coolgardie
disguise beats the gaudy speculator’s previous
record in a trot. The possibilities of travel and
adventure expressed by that brand-new get-up are
beyond human calculation. A party of friends,
whom Paddy called together for the purpose of
beholding him “ with ’em all on ” and wishing him
bon voyage, agreed that the knickerbockers alone
were equal to 15 journeys round the world. They
also considered that hostile natives would fall
paralysed before Paddy’s lawless head-gear.
Dear Bulletin, — Be “tight places.” A
workless miner at Eaglehawk, some years ago,
thought to pick up the run of stone in a certain
abandoned shaft, and, without telling anyone of
his intentions, proceeded to explore the old work-
ings. He had delved in the adjoining mine, but
had never been in the one he was now going to
“fossick.” Climbing down to the 400-feet level,
candle-in-hand, he explored crosscuts and drives,
and scrambled down winzes; but, in jumping a
bit of a “lump,’’ the jolt put his candle out and,
to his dismay, he found he hadn’t a match to re-
light it. As an old miner he should have told
his wife where he was going, in case of accident;
and, of course, he was to blame for not seeing to
his “ strikes” ere going down. It is to his credit
that he did not lose his presence of mind. That,
as he afterwards said, would have meant death
for certain. Crawling on hands and knees, and
keeping straight ahead, he went the fearful
journey slowly, but surely, down the “pass,”
through the drives, out in the crosscuts, in a dark-
ness that could be felt, and in an hour he reached
the shaft—to see the small circle of light 400 feet
overhead. To this day I have never seen him
strike a match if anyone was striking one to light
a pipe, or if a fire was handy. “Matches are
precious, my lad, I can tell you.”— Willow Tea.

A beautiful story of Christian presence of
mind comes from St. Paul’s Cathedral (Melb.).
The father of one of the chief chorister-boys, after
repeatedly expressing scorn for the- loose official
management of boy matters in general, told his
offspring to bid the organist farewell, and leave
word for his masters and pastors that he wouldn’t
be allowed to praise Heaven any more under the
Cathedral roof. The boy did as directed, after
singing at Dean Macartney’s funeral. He gave
the tabernacle five minutes’ warning, and went
back to the care of his wicked parents to bette r
himself. When the reverend precentor became
convinced that the missing lamb was really off for
good, he consulted the Cathedral Chapter-he
couldn’t consult the Dean for obvious reasons.
Two days later the father of the chorister who
had (under parental instructions) “chucked” the
blessed Chapter received a letter, announcing that
the Chapter felt compelled to dispense with the
emancipated warbler’s services, in consequence
etc. Which was tantamount to, say, Patterson
rising in the Vic. Assembly to state that he had
decided upon the dismissal of his vanished
Ministry.

■J^OTICE
FORECLOSURE
UNDER
REAL
PROPERTY
ACT.
In the matter of the application of the Mutual Life Association of Australasia, mortgagee under
memorandum of mortgage from Charles Grant, No. 184,007, dated 14th August, 1891, over the
whole of the land comprised in Certificate of Title, vol. 1030, folio 54.
WHEREAS the abovenamed mortgagee has made application for an order for foreclosure of the
mortgage above referred to, accompanied by such evidence of default for six months and other facts, as
required by section 113 of the Real Property Act: Notice is hereby given, pursuant to the said Act,
that the land comprised within the said mortgage, as hereunder particularised, is offered and will
remain open for sale until the 7th December, 1894, which date has been appointed as the time upon or
after which I, the Registrar-General, will issue to the applicant an order for foreclosure, which will
exclude all claim by the abovenamed mortgagor, and of any person claiming through or under
her, unless in the interval a sufficient amount has been realised by the sale of such land to satisfy
the principal and interest moneys due, and all costs and expenses occasioned by such proceedings, and
interest up to the time of such sale.
street, Sydney. , _ . _ „ _
Dated at Sydney this 26th day of October, 1894, at the Registrar-General’s Department.
CHARLES PINHEY. Registrar-General.

With an ordinary tape measure determine the width
round the joints AA, round the instep 88, round heel CO,
round the ankle DD ; next ascertain the length and width
of tread by placing the foot on a sheet of paper, and
running a lead pencil round the heel and toes. Cut the
outline thus made with a scissors, and enclose with the
other measurements, stating what size you generally take,
what are your peculiarities—a weak ankle, perhaps a pet
corn, ingrowing nails, or a blooming bunion. All thsss
little matters attended to, if you have a long thin foot, or
a short thick one, with very high instep, send to ELLIS’S,
where you are sure to get a fit. The proprietor, with a
view of introducing his new and simple system into the
country districts where practical boot making is unknown,
has decided to supply bis patrons at the lowest cash price.

On a certain Australian diggings was a
miner who had “ God strike me blind 1 ” con-
tinually on his tongue. One night, playing
Yankee-grab, he so sickened his mates with the
phrase that, when he fell asleep, they planned a
fright for him. When he showed signs of waking
they put the lights out and went on shaking and
counting, as if still playing, until from the sofa
came a horrified voice—“ God strike me blind, if
He hasn’t gone an’ done it 1 ”
Wool and Produce Reports.
M'Bdrnby, Wallis and Oo.’s Report.— At our wool sale
to-day competition was very keen, but prices cannot be
quoted any higher than last auctions. The low rates now
ruling are being accepted by growers as the only reason-
able alternative open to them, and consequently we were
able to clear our catalogue, with the exception of two
bales, which we protected up to our valuation by passing
in. The outlook in the immediate future is still very
gloomy, and we think growers will gain nothing by holding.
The London market is no better than our own, and consider-
ing that it costs about IJd. per lb. to ship and sell in Lon-
don, we would strongly advise our friends to make the best
of the bad times and sell in Sydney. Among the clips we
sold were the following; FG, a well got up clip from The
Rock, at 6Jd. ; DY, from same district, at 6Jd. ;
TP/DUBBO, a nicely-grown wool, at 6Jd. ; AP/New Eng-
land, a nice free wool, at 6Jd.; P over —/Temora, at6Jd.;

Song: By the Tarra.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
The light from sunset hill a-waning,
A low wind in the trees complaining
(The rippling river gleams along) ;
The golden day so hardly dying,
Gold shafts across the water lying,
The rustling reeds’ reluctant sighing
(The wandering river sings its song).
The bells of evening, rising, falling,
The sad-voiced plover’s plaintive calling
(The ruddy ripples glance along);
Far bells from out the city ringing.
And distance-mellowed voices singing,
Faint memories of the past are bringing
(The waters dancing on and on).
But, hark ! the last bell tolling, tolling,
The darkening water rolling, rolling
(The sullen ripples opening on) ;
A new-born moon, cloud-pillowed, dreaming,
And faintest star-shine gleaming, gleaming
To stars beneath the tide-way seeming
(The unseen river glooms along).
Low in the east a pale moon brightening,
Low in the west the cloud-wrack lightening
'(The humming water lapses on) ;
The solace of the night comes stealing,
Its balm and bliss afresh revealing
To sad hearts, sick for nature’s healing
(The silver ripples foam along).
So, too, our lives are flowing, flowing,
To the deep ocean onward going
(As runs the river ceaselessly);
On Time’s unresting currents shifting,
By moon or sun or starlight drifting,
To the fierce tide-race tossing, lifting
The drowning swimmers out to sea.

Dear Bulletin, —What I admire most
about the heathen Chinee is his coolness in emer-
gency. My Chinee is a cook on a river-steamer.
It was his first day aboard, and he wanted water.
A bucket was heaved overboard, and, as we were
going eight knots, the Chinkie nearly followed.
“Oap’n,” he said, some time afterwards, “if you
was welly nigh been dlagged off boat by bucket,
what would you do —let bucket go or hang on?”
“Why, let it go, of course, John,” replied the
skipper, “And, Cap’n,” said the heathen, his
face one huge smile, “that jes’ what I did.” —
Bilk.

Few faces and forms (says the Murchison
Times) are more familiar on the Murchison gold-
field than those of herculean “ Russian Jack ”
(Ivan Fredericks), native of Archangel, in far-
away Northern Russia. Jack is generally re-
garded as the strongest man on the Murchison.
Roughly speaking, he is about 43 years of age,
stands 6ft., and has a chest measurement of about
48in. He is strong as a lion, and, like another
big fellow —Prof. Miller, of Melbourne—is light-
hearted, and bubbling over with good nature.
Once on a time Jack started for the Kimberley
goldfields—about 300 miles’ journey—walking him-
self and wheeling on a barrow about 2cwt. of
tucker, tools, and other necessaries for a prospect-
ing tour. He pushed his barrow at the rate of 30
miles per day, and when within 70 or 80 miles of
the rush he was making for, he overtook a poor
chap who was humping his swag, and limping
along footsore and almost ready to drop. Jack
took in the situation at once. “ Shove your swag
on the top of mine,” he cheerfully said, and he
trundled away—the other man toiling on slowly
behind. Next day, the other man said he could
not keep up, and asked to be left behind to get on
as best he could. “No fear,” said Jack, and he
picked up the other man, put him also on top of
the already heavy load, and away he went. He
did his usual 30 miles that day, and the next, and
got there all the same.

FOUNTAINS IN PLAY.
Who doesn’t enjoy looking at a fountain in play?
•Ilia bright water leaps into the air as though ib
were a living, conscious thing. Then at the
summit of its arch it is shattered into spray by
the wind, or wafted to and fro like the skirts of a
dancing girl. And all the while the roar of its
rush and the tinkling melody of its fall fill the air.
Inis is water having a, good time—water in high
spints. What makes it jump, sing, and laugh in
■n^i/ aS non ‘ Wait a bit. If you please, we
will have the explanation later on. Perhaps you
know already ; perhaps not. It isn’t so easy to
know things from the bottom.
Under date of March 16th, 1892, a lady writes
that for twenty years she always felt low-spirited.
Now, what is meant by “ spirits ” in this sense is
a condition of the mind in respect of our being
cheerful, happy, enjoying our surroundings, taking
delight in all sorts of minor matters, and, in a word,
making the most of life ourselves and helping
others to make the most of it. Well then, it is
both a glorious and a profitable thing to be always
in good spirits ; I wish I could be myself.
But I can’t; anyhow, I’m not. Neither is
anybody. Yet why not ? What makes our hearts
sink down like a plummet, and then rise like a
cork ?
Before we answer let us read the rest of the
letter. The writer says: “Eor twenty years I
have suffered from liver complaint and indigestion.
1 was constantly tired, weak, and languid. My
skin was a sallow colour, and I had great pain at
the back and shoulders.
My appetite was poor, I had a bad taste in the
mouth, and much pain and weight at the chest
after eating. I was also frequently sick, throwing
up a sour, frothy fluid. I took different kinds of
medicine, but none of them gave me relief, and I
got weaker and weaker.
“In March, 1884, I became so bad that I called
a doctor, who attended me off and on for twelve
months. He gave me medicines, but I grew no
better, as nothing he prescribed seemed to reach
my complaint. About this time I heard of Mother
Seigel’s Syrup, and commenced taking it. In less
than a month all my pain and distress ceased, and
I have since been in good health. I keep a bottle
of the Syrup in the house as a family medicine,
and by taking an occasional dose have needed no
doctor.
“I may mention that my aunt, who lives at
West Wycombe, Bucks, was afflicted with indiges-
tion and dyspepsia. She states that in her opinion
Mother Seigel’s Syrup has been the means of
saving her life. You are at liberty to use this
statement as you may see fit. Yours truly,
(Signed) Mrs. Harriett Cutler. Burton House’
33 Kenmure Road, Hackney, London.”
. No philosopher has yet been able to say what
is the exact relation of the mind to the body.
This much, however, experience constantly assures
us of; that it is very intimate, and that the
condition of the mental faculties depends upon
that of the body. As birds sing when the sun
shines, and are dumb in the dark, so the spirits
and mental powers rise and express themselves
when we are in health, and are dull and torpid
when the body is under the weight of pain and
disease. And to know that is to know all we
require to act upon.
Everything, money, influence, and every form
of success depends so largely upon health that by
universal consent we don’t expect good service
from those who are ill.
And illness, nine times in ten, means precisely
what Mrs. Cutler suffered for such a long and
weary period—indigestion and dyspepsia—the
ailment cured (when the doctors are done with
their fruitless experiments) by Mother Seigel’s
Curative Syrup.
The fountains play only when the water is
forced powerfully through their pipes by gravity
or by machinery. So the spirits are free and
elastic only when the organs within us act without
hindrance or obstruction.
The Price of Truth.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
At every step in the search after truth a man tramples
on his own heart.—Olive Schreiner.
I have had none to say unto me ; “ Lo.!
This way, or that way, thou shalt turn and go.
“Thou hast the strength of soul that sets men
free,
The ways the thousands go are not for thee.
“ Thou shalt go down through wastelands dim
and vast,
Untrammelled by the thoughts of all the past—•
“ The past that grips the present as it stands,
And drags it downward with its ghastly hands.
“Thou shalt go out where’er thy light may
lead,
And in the lonely night thy heart shall bleed j
“Till, in the world around, the skies above,
Thou shalt not find nor tenderness nor love ;
“Nor evidence of system, nor of plan,
In all the universe, nor care for man.
“And, though thy heart shalt fail, and thou
shalt yearn
To feel thy fellows’ hands, thou canst not turn.
“But thou shalt see betimes, beside thy way,
The bones of those who went, like thee, astray.
“ And though thou criest, ‘ O God ! the whence
or why !’
Thou shalt not hear an echo of thy cry,”
Brisbane.

Lost in the Bush.
‘ Any adult male who is * lost in the bush ’ must
either be of defective intellect or incapable of
applying his intelligence practically,” says Mr.
Sharp, L.S., in S.M. Herald. There is some
truth in this, but it requires sifting. People who
“lose ” themselves generally get flurried, and are,
therefore, for the time being, incapable of practi-
cally applying their intelligence. Then the ques-
tion becomes a matter of physical endurance
whether they can hold out until they find
themselves again. There is scarcely any
rule possible to lay down, men’s tempera-
ments vary so. Most adult males know,
without teaching, that when a man is east
of a river he must go west to look for it again, but
there are cases where one might go astray even
in doing that. Take many portions of the interior
where the rivers are intermittent, i.e., lose their
defined channels for a time. Supposing a man
struck one of these gaps, he might go west until
further orders looking for that river. Again, take
Western Australia in parts where there are
no rivers, creeks, nor leading features, and a good
deal of scrub. On a dull day, without a compass,
the ordinary man minus the special instinct of
locality would stand a very bad show, no matter
what precepts had been instilled into him. Cer-
tainly every man should carry a pocket-compass,
as it would give him confidence that he was going
in a straight line ; and, so far as N.S. Wales and
Victoria are concerned, he could not go long on a
straight line without coming to something. The
prominent stars, as well as the sun and moon, are,
of course, good guides; and most people know the
prominent stars by sight, even if not by name.
Bushmanship, as an instinctive knowledge of
locality is called, is undoubtedly as much a gift as
music or painting. You can no more make
bushmen of some men, although they live in the
bush all their lives, than you can make a musician
of a man who has not the natural aptitude.
Education will do little in the matter, otherwise
it would not be so notorious that surveyors (!)
are usually bad bushmen. Then, again, we
have such instances as Leichhardt and Lands-
borough, who were both deficient in the gift.
One man can recognise such an object as a clump
of timber, a particular rock, &0., even in the dark,
although only once seen, and another might pass
it twenty times and not remember it. To some
the bush is all alike; to others there are not two
yards the same. To keep cool, to keep a straight
line, and carry a pocket-compass is about the only
advice to give to the general public; to go into
details would only “ bother ” men who, after all,
would not understand it any more than the non-
musician understands anything but simple tunes.
DELOOMFN
Australian papers hold up Amir Abd-u-
Raliman as “the ally of Britain.” He is, for a
substantial consideration in the shape of an annual
pension which alone has enabled him to keep his
seat on the CHbuli throne. The idea that Abd-u-
Rahrnan’s incoherent sovereignty is a “buffer”
between Russia and India is one of those political
superstitions ingrained in the Jingo mind. Oabul
could oppose no effective resistance to a Russian
advance, simply because it would fall to pieces at
the first touch. The men of the tribes are brave
enough, but as soon as the pressure from
was removed, they would begin fighting among
themselves. Afghanistan is itself a superstition—
a mere geographical expression for a country
inhabited by a miscellaneous collection of moun-
tain tribes, whose territory has been pierced a
score of times since Alexander’s day by the in-
vaders of India.

Peacock, Yic. Chief Sec. and Education
Minister, is a remarkably free-and-easy youth.
He wanders round his office, hands in pockets
and a big pipe in his mouth. During his first
week the caretaker of the old Treasury, a big
Irishman, came upon the jaunty Minister with
his pipe in full blast, as usual. “Phwat the
are yez smokin’ here fur? ” roared the blusterous
Cerberus. “Come inside, and I’ll tell you,”
suavely replied Peacock, as he disappeared into
the Minister’s holy of holies. That caretaker is
now sorry he spoke.

Stepheniana .
In its picturesque and dramatic character, the life
of ex-Ohief Justice Alfred Stephen, of.N.S.W,,
stands pre-eminent in the remarkable record of
the many individualities which have distinguished
the brief page of Australian history. Consider
that tliis man who but a day or two agone was
carried to his grave, knew Wilberforce, and had
heard Canning; had listened in private to the
chastened eloquence which had freed the slave,
and in the halls of the Senate to that embellished
oratory which, transmuted into action, had
“ created a new world to redress the balance of
the old !” Consider this one simple fact, and
grasp, if you can, the tremendousness of the moral
and political revolutions which were embraced
within the span of that one man’s life !
• * •
Fertile in incident such a life must necessarily
have been when that life linked the England of
the first decade of the century and the Australia
of the last. Episodes to furnish motives for fifty
average novels can be chosen from the “ storied
tissue ”of such an experience. Here are two or
three which pulse with the emotion of human
stress and storm, taken from the tale of his early
colonial years.
* # *
The Hobart Town Press in the early twenties
was official, and therefore the reverse of “free.”
It was as literally “under bond” as a newly-
arrived transport with a life-sentence. Then fear-
less expression of opinion adverse to the authorities
was impossible without risk of the forfeiture o(
liberty or goods. Young Crown-Solicitor Stephen
was, no doubt conscientiously, opposed from the
first to freeing the Press from its restrictions. He
smote with legal weapons the few rare spirits who
dared to censure him. Melville, Bent, Savary—
these three felt the force of his law-strengthened
arm. Savary was a convict whose slight chance
of freedom would have been altogether lost had
legal evidence been forthcoming to convict him of
the authorship of a certain published letter.
He was suspected of. the authorship, but proof
could not be obtained which would satisfy the
exactness of Stephen’s judgment. But, failing the
writer, the publisher could be punished. And
punished Melville would have been to the loss of
all property but for a deed creditable to both the
Crown Solicitor and the convict.
• • »
One night, Mr. Stephen was called from his
drawing-room to the verandah of his cottage. “ A
pris’ner wished to see him.” Demurring at first-
most irregular was it for a great official then to be
interviewed by a man under bond—he at last
consented. The “prisoner” was Savary, or
Savery—formerly a rich sugar-boiler, then Mayor
of Bristol, later a condarane to the gallows, later
still, as we see him now, a transport for life.
“Do I speak, sir, to Mr. Stephen the man, or
to Mr. Stephen the Crown Solicitor?” asked
Savary, saluting as by regulation compelled.
“ I cannot separate myself from my office,” said
Mr. Stephen.
“Not even to do a kindly, a generous thing,
your Honor ? ”
“lean make no conditions,” replied the law-
officer.
Savary saluted again, apologised for troubling
His Honor, but said, as he turned to go,
Punish Mr. Melville, sir, for printing that
letter and you ruin an innocent man. I wrote
the letter, and will say so in Court.”
He went, believing that his one chance for a
conditional pardon was thus forfeited.
aic ol )e , nex * ) da y, proceedings were stayed.
Irred otephen was touched by the convict’s mag-
nanimity, and for once, at least, dissociated the
man from the official.
* * *
Bent libelled Stephen—though now it does not
seem a very terrible libel. But the Governor in-
sisted that certain action should be taken. It
was taken, and Bent was ruined. Years after-
wards, Bent wished to make a fresh start in
oydney. ihe first five pounds subscribed to the
tuna in aid of his project was contributed by
Judge Stephen. J
* * •
Atone Assize in Launceston, Attorney-General
Stephen prosecuted to conviction 78 capital
offenders, of whom 30 were hanged.
* * *
Distinguished lawyer as Alfred Stephen proved
to be, it is curious to learn that in his early
professional years he hated the law. A diary lies
before me as I write, which is the record of a
woman’s life in Australia and Tasmania from 1830
to 1865. Ihe lady was the wife of an officer who
arrived with his regiment at Hobart Town in the
early “ thirties.” “The night of my arrival,” she
writes, “I danced with the Attorney-General of
the colony at a ball given by the Governor. He
was a charming companion, and a good dancer,
but amused me greatly by regretting that he had
been trained to the law. It was his ambition to
become an upholsterer ! ”
* • •
Alfred Stephen’s first case in the colonies was
one in which he figured as plaintiff. He sued the
captain of the vessel which brought him and his
wife to Van Diemen’s Land for non-fulfilment of
contract to provide suitable aboardship accommod-
ation. He won the case. The man was his own
lawyer here, but he had no fool for his client.
* * *
Among the assigned servants in Hobart Town
was a man who had been one of Stephen’s school-
mates, The fellow was an unsurpassably in-
genious scoundrel, and frequently obtained goods
from tradesmen on his master’s account. It fell to
Stephen’s lot to prosecute him. The jury was
a military one of five officers. “Gentlemen,”
said the prisoner, “ don’t let the Crown Solicitor
have it upon his conscience that he hanged a man
who had been his companion in boyhood’s innocent
hours. Don’t spare me, gentlemen, but consider
Mr. Stephen!” The jury laughed, the Court
smiled, Mr. Stephen remained for a moment
silent, and then said simply: “Gentlemen, your
oath !” And the man was found guilty.
* * »
At a time when everyone almost in official
circles frowned upon Captain Maconochie’s
attempts to reform the most heinous criminals by
lenient methods, Alfred Stephen was the one
prominent public official who, without absolutely
accepting all that Maconoehie planned, aided him
by encouraging words. “Do your best,” he
wrote, “even though you fail.”
* • #
Thomas Griffiths Wainwright, the notorious
poisoner, the friend of Charles Lamb and Hazlitt,
the terrible original of Varney in Bulwer
Lytton’s “ Lucretia,” and the villain in Dickens’
“Hunted Down,” painted a portrait for Mr.
Stephen. At the time Wainwright was a wards-
man in the general hospital at Hobart, and
reports were current as to the brutality of his
treatment of the patients in his charge. Mr.
Stephen cautioned him to be careful of his con-
duct. “Sir,” replied Wainwright, “men of cul-
ture and refinement such as you and I should
forget that we stand to each other in any other
relation than that of artist and patron !”
# * •
A man was indicted ■ by Attorney-Gen oral
Stephen for forging a promissory-note of one J.
Weavell for the sum of one shilling. “It is not
the value of the sum thus fraudulently obtained,”
declared the law-officer to the jury, “ that has to
be regarded, but the crime of forgery.” The man
was hanged. Here we have the stern sense of
duty in the prosecuting barrister, which, in the
J udge, became an apparently merciless severity.
* * #
In the first two-volume novel ever printed in
Australia, and possibly the first ever written,
Alfred Stephen was introduced as one of the
characters. The author was Savary abovemen-
tioned, and the book was “Quintus Servinton.”
Savary died a suicide.
« » 9
W. H. Barber, the heroic solicitor, who was
transported in 1844 for forgery, and subsequently
proved innocent, found a friend in Stephen—then
Judge in New South Wales. Stephen initiated
the subscriptipn which sent Barber to Paris (as he
was not permitted to set foot in the United
Kingdom for some time) so that he might con-
vince the British authorities of his innocence.
After a struggle of 20 years’ duration, Barber
succeeded, only to die. One of his last letters
was to Alfred Stephen. At the great Assize
that letter may be safely trusted to balance the
errors of even so long a life.
One of the less admirable episodes in the dead
jurist’s career was his treatment of Gellibrand,
Attorney-General at the time Mr. Stephen arrived
in V.D. Land. Mr. Stephen was, as a law-
officer, liable to have his course of action over-
ridden by Mr. Gellibrand. Gellibrand was a man
of high character, but imperious in temper.
Stephen had all the aggressiveness of a young
man, which was rather heightened than modified
by the belief that as he came of a legal family he
was the possessor of inherited legal acumen. No
wonder, therefore, they quarrelled. The fault
was unquestionably with the younger man, and
he carried the vendetta to a point which, in after
years, he must have looked upon regretfully.
Gellibrand’s infirmity of temper accentuated the
trouble; and Arthur, Lieutenant-Governor, a
vindictive-spirited administrator, had had his own
quarrel with Gellibrand and supported Stephen.
# * «
The contest thus ended with Gellibrand’s dis-
comfiture. He left office a disappointed man.
But —and here is why the incident is now referred
to—as so often happens, out of private wrong
cometh public good, Gellibrand, finding Van
Diemen’s Land too small a sphere for his enter-
prising spirit and untiring industry, conceived a
plan for the exploration and pastoral settlement of
Port Phillip (Victoria). He it was who organised
the association which sent out John Batman, the
founder of Victoria, He it was who drew up the
memorandum of agreement among the subscribers.
He it was who supplied the organising, con-
trolling energy of the movement, and who carried
his devotion to the future of the enterprise to the
extent that he laid down his life for it. Per-
aonally visiting the settlement to explore “the
new country,” he, with a companion, Hesse, set
out one morning to traverse “ the fresh woods and
pastures new ” of the district extending from
Geelong to Birregurra. They were never seen by
white men again. The blacks killed them—and,
it is a traditional belief in Western Victoria—ate
them.
* * •
Thus Mr. Stephen’s action was directly the
cause of Victoria’s foundation —at all events, but
for him the settlement of the colony would not
have taken place till a later date.
* * •
In 1892, the British Museum purchased for =£2s,
in London, one of the duplicates of an agreement
drawn up between John Batman and Jagga-
Jagga, the chief of the Dutigalla tribe of abori-
gines, whose camping-ground was the present site
of Melbourne. The other copy is now jealously
preserved in the Melbourne Public Library. Ge(-
librand drafted and engrossed the documents, but
he was never aware that one of his co-members of
the Association had submitted the draft con-
fidentially to Alfred Stephen, or that one of the
suggestions he, Gellibrand, accepted and embodied
with the final copy, was made by the man he
regarded as his bitter enemy.
* # #
As to Judge Stephen’s opposition to the reprieve
of Mary Brownlow, had the Stephen Divorce Act
of to-day been then in operation, the chances are
that that poor woman would, long before the occur-
rence of the event which prompted the desperate
deed for which she was hanged, have secured a
divorce from her brutal husband. In a casual
discussion of the Brownlow case, after the passage
of the Divorce Act, the ex-Judge had this put
before him. It was, perhaps, unnecessary to do
so. For it is pretty certain that in the Brownlow
case was the germ of the great enactment of his
last legislative years.
* * *
It was in connection with the Brownlow case
that a dramatic incident occurred. Special
arrangements had been made by the coach-pro-
prietors to carry what was believed to be the
reprieve from Sydney to Goulburn. Everyone
put forth strenuous efforts to reach the scene of
the execution before the dread climax. Surely
there could be no doubt the document so urgently
transmitted was the commutation order! But—
it was the death warrant! The driver of the
coach (a hotel-keeper at Gundagai) afterwards told
the writer that had he known the true nature of
the paper he would have lost the mail.
• * #
Alfred Stephen was a contributor to the Tas-
manian Gazette when Dr. Ross edited it. He
also wrote_ colonial letters to the Morning
Chronicle in London, and in later years his con-
tributions were published in the Australian,
Atlas, and Herald (Sydney). He wrote a
remarkably clear hand—and thus was a delight to
compositors. In Sydney, in 1848, a compositor
in the Atlas office, then edited by James
Martin, afterwards Stephen’s successor in the
Chief Justiceship, received a slip of “copy” to
set up; The “ copy ” was that of a satirical poem
to which no signature was attached, “ Whoever
wrote that,’’ exclaimed the printer, “used to
write from Van Diemen’s Land to the Chronicle
when I was in that office. There was one letter
I remember which, when it reached Australia,
must have made trouble for somebody.” The
verses were written by Stephen.— “ One Who
Knew Him,” in Windsor (N.S.W.) Gazette

ALMOST.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
It was not in wooded valley, by the springing year
made gay,
With the zephyr softly sighing through the golden
wattle-spray ;
Nob in gorgeous planted garden ’mid exotic
beauties rare,
Nor by peaceful river scented with the fragrant
forest air.
There was nob a thing romantic in that sombre,
sober spot,
Save the sad sprig, full of meaning, of the blue
forget-me-not.
It was off a city roadway, in a cheerless, dismal
room,
That a lovely form lay dying in the hideous lethal
gloom;
I had loved but had nob won her, though she
loved me passing well,
And though since she bade me leave her, all her
life was earthly hell.
If our loving had but linked us fair our lives had
been and bright,
But her thoughts were queenly ever, and I knew
my love was right.
I remembered how, a few years back, I’d clasped
in fond embrace
That divinely-minded woman, finest type of all
How her eager lips with mine had met in passion’s
wildest kiss
When I dreamt our souls were gliding into life-
long lovers’ bliss.
How ray every fibre tingled as we clung there
breast to breast,
As I felt the passioned grasping of the fingers that
I pressed.
’Twas in hot beseeching accents that I asked her
if with me
She would leave that house of torture for a home
beyond the sea.
Would she cast away the sorrow of her fearful
married life;
Would she kneel at God’s high altar and become
my lawful wife.
For my love had wed a tyrant who bub made her
fret and fear,
Who was common-place 'and brutal —most repul-
sive mate for her.
Then she raised her warm web eyelids, and I
paused to hear her choice;
There was music low and mournful in her sweet
and trembling voice:
“ Though I loathe my hated husband with his
heartless cutting sneer,
I have borne unto him children, and I hold those
children dear;
And the blood I gave my offspring ne er shall rise
in blush of shame .
When the hard world in derision speaks their
loving mother’s name.’
Then I told her that those children would be mine
across the sea,
But her woman’s instinct shuddered at the hinted
infamy.
“No, dear, no; before the great world purest
wifely love I’ll feign,
That the name I leave my darlings may be left
them free from stain.”
All those years in lonely longing she had lived the
bounden lie;
Since that night we had not spoken till I came to
see her die.
Then the smile with which she met ,me showed a
mind at last at rest;
There was naught of fevered passion in the fingers
that I pressed.
She had fought the fight and conquered, but at
what a frightful cost 1
For we counted no hereafter and her earthly joy
was lost.
Have our pious modern Christians such unselfish
Christ-like care ?
God Almighty ! what a torment may thy noblest
creatures bear.
Melbourne.
K.B.
Bush Orgies and Fighting.
Dear Bulletin,— Re your quotation from that
distinguished Australian explorer, Guy Boothby,
of a bush ball at Cloncurry, I wish to take exoep-
tion to the idea engendered. I am sorry to say
that, to many “Australian” authors, the revelry
of a bush pub. is a scene of wild and dangerous
single combats. Men fight in Australia just as
much, and no more, than they do in other coun-
tries. The ordinary drunken bush-hand usually
starts by calling his opponent a something “liar !”
The other retorts by calling him an embellished
liar!!” and so it goes on until one or the other’s
vocabulary of adjectives is exhausted. Perhaps
one may emphasise his defiance by smashing his
thick-bottomed tumbler on the counter, and being
promptly charged two-and-six by the landlord for
breakage; but there will be no fight. Of course,
there are scrapping matches, and one or two may
come down, making clumsy rushes; but no real
fighting. I and two others once camped at a
bush pub., after being up nearly two nights
watching rowdy cattle. It was raining cats
and dogs, the cattle had been delivered and
yarded, so we thought we were due for a good
night’s sleep under shelter. It was in the neigh-
borhood of one of the earliest of the N.Q. gold-
fieids, and there were about twenty or more men
drinking in the house, a common bush shanty
w herein every whisper was audible. They kept it
up until morning, and, to an innocent new-chum
Q- U y Boothby, no doubt it would seem that a
pandemonium of carnage was raging, but there
wag no fighting, and no one seemed to expect a
fight. Every time I awoke—for I slept through
KtK
»•»* “ “f rav < 4 r “ h ’’ in the morning-
through having fallen on the floor on their faces,
but that was the worst. As I said before, men
fight no more and no less here than m other
countries. In the course of a far-back bush ex-
penence of many years, in four of the five pro-
-o„|y morning; fought scientifically to a finish,
and—in one case—an almost fatal finish. No !
the bush hand when drunk may rend the air with
lurid blasphemy, but there is really little of this
“going into the backyard business,” so popular
with modern bush authors. There is any amount
of tall blowing a few days afterwards, and the man
with the biggest voice, who has called the others the
biggest number of “favorite liars,” has many stories
of his gory prowess to relate—and there it stops.
As for bush balls 1 I never saw one on the Clon-
curry, nor—in point of fact —do I think there
have been many balls held there, for obvious
reasons ; but I have seen bush balls in many other
Queensland townships in the rough-and-ready
days, and 1 must confess that I do not recognise
Mr. Boothby’s description. It certainly is like
many I have seen written up by men calling them-
selves Australians, but nothing more than a dim
resemblance to it has ever come under my notice.
Every girl, high and low, has a love of “side”
inherent in her, and the lower her station the
more she would be likely to insist upon attention
to little social details. Of course I do not refer
to an abandoned woman. As to what I said about
the non-fighting of the bush-hand when drunk, be
it understood that I have no experience of a navvy’s
camp, where, I believe, a good deal of brutal
fighting goes on.
BINGHI.
Judge Casey, of Yic. County Court, culti-
vates the courteous and urbane, and pulled up
counsel, who was conducting a cross-examination,
for conduct he termed “ ungentlemanly.” Counsel
retorted that he was unaware his conduct was so.
His Honor: “Your inability in that direction is a
matter of regret to the court and sorrow to your
friends. Let the case proceed.’*
George Bates, lately admitted to S.A.
Destitute Asylum, settled on Kangaroo Island
(S.A.) in 1824, 13 years before S.A. was founded.
He originally came to Australia as seaman on a
convict ship in 1822. Bates lived with the natives
on the S.A, mainland for years, and the first
settlers who arrived from England were much
astonished when they were welcomed by a white
man with a numerous brood of half-castes. Asked
recently by a pressman what was his most excit-
ing adventure, old George replied, “ Well—l think
the nearest shave I ever had was when I got
drunk at Port Adelaide and walked straight out of
Joe Barney’s pub. into the river 1”
S.M. Herald prints some very good lead-
ing articles now, and is broadening surprisingly.
Apropos a shrieky evangelist’s suggestion that, in
the plain and holy language of Scripture, no man
can see a woman in evening dress without lusting
after her, the Herald goes so far as to say :
The teaching of history and experience is that the
asoetio ideal, by its impracticability and its ignoring a
large and indestructible part of our nature and our life,
has at times done much more to depreciate the average
standard of morality than to raise it.
This is a definite acceptance of the creed that
“self-development is higher than self-sacrifice,”
that man doesn’t want salvation, only educa-
tion ; and it thus amounts to a definite rejec-
tion of the cardinal dogma of all the Oriental
faiths, which preach that natural passions are
wrong and should be repressed and extirpated.
It is quite possible that in another 100 years or so
the Herald will denounce monarchy, priestcraft
and other shibboleths with all its might.
“ Warrigal ” writes to The Bulletin on a
chapter of N.S.W. bushranging history :
A correspondent of yours institutes a comparison
between cavalry-sword and revolver as effective weapons
for troopers, and satisfactorily proves, to himself, that
for offensive purposes the former instrument is the better
in such guerrilla warfare as that in which poor Sergeant
Wallings was engaged when he lost his life. Youroorres-
pendent’s facts, as he relates them, would, to some extent
substantiate his inference, but as his case is in the main
purely hypothetical, his conclusions, for all comparative
purpose, are valueless. I tell the story thus:—lt was in
78, after the big drought broke up, and “ information ’’
by the usual mysterious channel was conveyed to the
police that “Midnight,” as we knew him, would be at
“Mother Mill’s ” place on a certain date. Mother Mill’s
it should be interjected, was a pub. of the good old
variety that had its own little coterie of supporters who
appeared and disappeared in eccentric fashion, but always
turning up, mostly “ from Queensland,” at unsuspected
periods, and within a week or two of each other, to drink
gamble, and compare notes. “Midnight" was one of
these, and one of the most daring and unscrupulous of
them all. In the bush he was not less fearless, and night
or day, whatever the job, he could be depended upon to
do his share of it. The intelligence concerning his move-
ments was correct, and, as he was “wanted," Sergt. Wal-
lings himself undertook the service of capture, having
with him Sergt. Souter, of Warren, and P. 0. Walsh, of
Dubbo. I should explain that “ Mother Mill’s Place " was
on Wonbobbie, 16 miles from Warren. Wonbobbie is now in
the possession of Mr. Thos. Buckland, but was then owned
by Mr. H. Bird, son of Major Bird, and the handsomest
man in the North-West. On the night preceding the
tragedy, the police took up position and surrounded the
house. At daylight a demand for admission was made in
the usual terms. Immediately this alarm was given
“Midnight” was seen scurrying from the backhand
making toward a fence, 60 or 70 yards away. Wallings
rightly guessed this to be his man, took after him, arid
firing over his head—not at him, remember- called for a
surrender. Seeing the fence was unattainable, “Mid-
night " dropped behind a tree and, shouldering a Snider
levelled it straight at the sergeant, remarking, in low’
but distinct tones, “ Keep back, Wallings, or by God I’ll
put a bullet through you, for I’ll never be taken alive!”
The unfortunate officer, making some sort of laughing
reply about the “game being up” and the “ nonsense” of
resisting, blazed away mercifully high again, and continued
to advance. He was not 30ft. away when the rifle banged
and Wallings threw up his arms with a cry and fell from
his horse in a heap to the ground, mortally wounded in
the chest. Midnight then got through the fence un-
molested, and a few days later a blood stallion disappeared
from Warrana, Buchanan’s station, 60 miles away. The
police, of course, were up and out in all directions, but
Midnight’s track was never hit upon till “information”
was again sent in—by a woman, they say—that he would
endeavor to cross the Q. border on a certain day, vid
Eungonia. This was also “ straight wire,” and Constable
Grey, of Bourke, I think, with a blackfellow, getting on
the right track from a well-known wayback shanty, stuck
to it and came up with his quarry on the Warrego.
Grey played at long range for some time, when he first
brought down the horse and, finally, the man, planting,
it is said, nearly as much lead in both as Thunderbolt
carried to the tomb with him. As for the “ auxiliaries"
Wallings took with him, Souter, when he saw the nature
of the wound, immediately gallopped to Wonbobbie,
“for assistance” (his own words); while Walsh alleged that
he tried to make his horse jump the fence to go in pursuit
when y Midnight ” discharged another shot that took his
steed in the ear, which rendered the animal unmanage-
able for several miles up the road. For the rest, had
Wallings fired right at “ Midnight,” he would, in all pro-
bability, have hit and disabled the fellow, as he was more
than fairly good with pistol or revolver. As for the sword,
a broom-handle would have been just as useful. To the
last moment of his life, Wallings thought “ Midnight"
would not dare to fire at him.

Tales of the Austral Tropics.
“ The volume takes its name from one of the sketches, ‘ The Last of Six,’ though in point of merit they all stand pretty well on a par. The
author, who is familiar with life in Northern Queensland, writes with vigour about the doings of the whites and the natives in those faraway
parts. A strong vein ot mysticism runs through his narratives, and there are ghosts in almost every chapter, and for those who are fond of
realistic work the volume will prove attractive reading.”—S.M. Herald, July 8, 1893.
“ No writer has given the world a more vivid description of Australia and its wilderness than Ernest Favenc, and the secret of this is that
he has, by many years’ experience of travel and bush life, acquired a knowledge of this continent which is possessed by scarcely any other writer.
He, too, possesses great descriptive powers. Whatever he puts pen to is certain to be good. In the stories under notice he has given us some of
his best work. That they will be widely read is certain.”— Sydney Mail, July 8, 1893.
“They present with force and vividness, as well as with the accuracy arising from long contact and keen observation, striking pictures of
parts of Australia unfamiliar to most of its inhabitants ; they tell, sometimes with remarkable power, of incidents such as happen in wild places
when human hearts are stirred by strong passions, and of weird imaginings that move the minds of lonely men. ‘ The Last of Six ’ is a terrible
talc of cannibalism, treachery, and revenge upon the dead, which in other hands might be gross and hideous, but which Mr. h avenc makes of
thrilling interest. ‘ The Haunt of the Jinkarras’ suggests Rider Haggard and his imitators to some extent, but is itself relieved from the
suspicion of imitation by faithful local colour and a grotesquerie of conception, in which Mr. Favenc here and there approaches Poo. This
characteristic is especially noticeable in ‘Spirit Led,’ which the great American might have written. ‘A Cup of Cold Water’ is, perhaps, the
best tale in a collection that should be widely read, and which forms a worthy addition to purely Australian literature.”— Daily Telegraph
July 15, 1893.
“ ‘ The Last of Six ;’ and other stories. By Ernest Favenc. (Sydney : The Bulletin Newspaper Co ) This is a collection of short but
graphic sketches by Mr. Favenc, the well-known explorer, who has a rare collection of Australian legendary lore. All the stories here are good,
and « o can heartily endorse the recommendatory notice by Rolf Boldrewood prefixed to this collection. The present issue forms the third of The
Bulletin scries of Australian reminiscences.”— Australian Star, July 15, 1893.
